dirk visser 09:13:49 5/06/2004
Hi Brian,
I looked for Kahn but no luck so far. Did pick up a copy of Stewart Brand, "How Buildings Learn", though, and am enjoying it. There's a lot more wide ranging and detailed investigation of the supporting issues to building than I remembered, building codes, finance, real estate, preservation, maintainance, remodel, style immunity, and a lot of other interesting aspects to the built environment. Recommended.

"Cities are like gravitational sinks that suck buildings in and destroy them, almost nothing is exempt." Whew!

A telling story of cultural/institutional foresight at Oxford College in 1970,when the library beams put up 300 years ago were found to be infested with beetles. After a fruitless search of the lumber market someone finally thought to inventory college lands for trees big enough to mill relacement timbers from.
Upon inquiry the college forester replied, Well sirs, we was wonderin' when you'd be askin'."
A grove of Oaks had been planted when the hall was first built for the express purpose of providing these replacements 3 centuries later...

What about the next time? Had a new grove been planted in 1970 for use in the year 2400? Brand was asked this a few times so he called the college archivist and the clerk of Works, and asked. They had no idea. Maybe the foresters just took care of it.

Enjoy the Koi ponds, old man, ha, ha! d.v.

dirk visser

D.V. 21:41:21 5/04/2004
The Entropy thing is certainly worth a comment; interesting about the biotic vs. abiotic geometry, there's probably a lot in that one!

The Physicist is very proud of his discovery that the observable universe is degrading to lower and lower overall order, that the arrow of time only goes one way, the heat death of the universe etc.

He forgets to assign proportional importance to one key fact, not in dispute: That is that locally restricted increasing complexity and interconnection is found.

As the bulk matter of the universe cools and dissipates, local nodes of high intensity points of order are becoming etablished and building up. Biology is the classic example, but microcircuitry is coming up fast. Architecture's formative, geometrical, and organizational powers are deeply involved in making any exquisite order possible.

The existence of these high intensity points and their resultant ability to create high speed innovation, far outstrips the facts of of The Laws of Thermodynamics. The profound implications of this have not yet entered the mind set of contemporary science, but within it lies the fulcrum to begin to assign lasting values to our activities and discoveries, and to lay a footing, based on what we see happening in nature, for an ethic that transcends social belief or religious faith.

Good luck with that little project, Dirk

D.V.

I read in either Sacred Geometry by Lawler or The Mathematics of Art and Life by ? an interesting observation--that in the mineral world carbon atoms form hexagonal patterns, something to do with entropy, but in living matter, I think they said uniquely, you have the appearance of five-sided geometries, apparently due to the fact that life resists entropy in some way that results in this fundamental shift in forms. So I like to try to exploit this observation to put "more life" into a design. (Don't ask me how come our military center is a pentagon, I have no idea.)

brian kennedy 11:22:55 5/02/2004
Yo, Dirk
I think it is fun to think about questions like the ones you pose about "nature" and gradually accruing abilities in the human form for manipulation of the environment, or Rob's about how centers and memory could be related.
I do not have much faith in the results of trying to answer such questions by ordinary human beings, because of the limits of our abilities to even observe what is going on. For example, Adi Da Samraj has pointed out that because our nervous system "takes time" to report to the brain, there is a built-in lag in our perception process such that we are never actually conscious of whatever the present Reality is. In a way, we are just not in a position to comment! To get an accurate idea of the Reality of the room you may be in at the moment, you would need to enjoy a perception of all possible "points of view" of that room. Our words are generated in a field of myriads of uninspected limits on our perception. The self-limit is a profound fault. Delusion, as Buddhists say. So, the unenlightened "point of view" is not equipped to understand the answers to the questions it may try to cook up. It is the frog in the well oblivious to the world outside, or the insect with no comprehension of winter, to refer to some of Ih Tiao Chang's quotations. So to really deal with such questions, I feel you have to deal with overcoming to self-limit. Of course, entrusting the "ego" to deal with itself is like asking Nixon to deal with Watergate, it's a joke. So, if one is serious, I believe one ends up seeking out a Guru. How one, already out of one's depth, could evaluate such a Guru accurately seems like a tall, paradoxical order for the self-limit to figure out. But, the egoic mind is not the final arbiter. There is exstacy (from I think Greek for standing (stasis) outside (ex) the self.) There is Love, there is Humor. Adi Da Samraj has told us that Feeling is "the hole in the Universe."

As far as craft or Art always improving and raising the bar on itself, I think that probably is true, but it sounds exhausting to the ego to say it. Whereas, the process is actually "ecstatic," in the previously mentioned sense, and thus not really tiring at all in reality.
Nakashima's way of approaching it, as a woodworker, was to make the furniture about the wood, showing the qualities in the wood. So even if he made the same shape chair each time, the wood was always changing, so his craft could "hitch a ride" on that stream of energy--it didn't have to be his "mind" doing "all the work."

brian kennedy

Hey Dirk
Lloyd Kahn's new book is out--Homework. Kind of a continuation of Shelter--the one they did after Domebook 1 and two. Loaded with pictures of do it yourself houses, some great stuff.
There is a photo of a Russian wooden church, an ascending series of onion dome shapes in the roof. Legend has it that the architect-builder, Nestor, did it with just an axe.

I don't feel bound by concerns for cultural signals in colors or forms for my own building projects. I am just interested in what my own nervous system reports to me. I find if I can please myself, that other people are nearly always pleased, usually radically. Like when I intalled the most outrageous gate-railing combination I have yet managed to pull off, which is at 42 Winfield in San Francisco, (in case you are in the neighborhood) a passer-by got down on her knees in front of me and salomed! Of course she was kidding, but still, that's pretty strong from a complete stranger. If an artisan does their job, installation should initiate a period of "shock and awe." Especially considering that we are working in an environment that has become a wasteland due to degeneration of craft in building--it's pretty easy to blow their minds by putting more aesthetic effort into things.

D. Visser 15:08:32 5/01/2004
The color interface as we have internally codified the wavelength frequencies of light is certainly worthy of further curiosity.

The ancestral parents house, now a rental, is a one story hip roof bungalow. A plain Jane except for the somewhat strong medium green, with carbon black trim. The sunshade is around back, and the more I'm around it, viewing it from the jobsite at the adjoining house the more it recedes, assuming almost invisibility, another thing to ponder.

Is it fair to say we have represented the two presence of absence of light with black and white? Something or nothing, the most profound relationship possible?
Certainly they are not only extremes of a gradient on the spectrum like deep, deep purple darkness, and on the other end hot, red hot, then white hot?

Or maybe both?

And why only these colors, why not some we cannot presently imagine. Painters sometimes travel to the South of France or New Mexico, for the quality of the light, is this a clue that we are not seeing some hidden qualities in our local colors?

Nature signals with the black/white bands, it's true, generally advising caution, or warning. The police car shows the human recognition that it works. Way beyond this colors have a wide and subtle range of meaning in nature and culture, how did that evolve?
Color perception and implication is so internalized it becomes difficult to explain and impossible to look at with detachment, and the same goes for form.
This is one of Alexander's major obstacles, I think, in writing a "constitution" for beauty.
The sharp, spare, 90 degree top corner of the sunshade jars and disturbs, up close,alone, but from a distance with the whole house in the picture, a nicely rounded corner would jump out as incongrous extra care.
The perceptions and interpretations change depending on proximity and context. This consideration is in addition the matters of time, taste, style, refinement, ego involement, and quality you guys have already loaded on this poor little framework previously.
Maybe the interest has endured here because we can all be a bit superior and condescending about the whole matter so we comment from positions of strength.
I know that always gives me enough rope to hang myself.
Do something pagan, It's mayday, Dirk

D. Visser

brian kennedy 09:56:37 5/01/2004
keypadpals:
One interesting aspect of this example is it is black and white detail on a structure that I presume is not black and white. I happened to have to clear two rattlesnakes from the yard right around that time and one thing about them in this area is they have, in a short section for a few inches just before the rattles, a beautiful section of black and white alternating bands which contrasts nicely with their grey or brown camoflage body pattern. The mosquitos around here also have b+w banding on their legs, I think. B+W is a snappy attention grabbing detail, a highlight that can look pretty cool as a surprise. So a little B+W detail, maybe a little incongruous, but maybe not. Maybe rolling with the same pattern that is rolling thru the rattlesnakes and mosquitoes.
There is a lizard around here called the Blue-tailed Skink which has a brown body with yellow stripes running towards the tail, which suddenly becomes a very beautiful, spectacular blue. In seems like such an odd combination of colors, yet the lizard is very beautiful. It is an interesting lesson to me as to "what 'Nature' can 'get away with' in terms of combining colors I would not expect could successfully go together well. Little do I know, of course! I wondered if this color move was prompted by a Strange Attractor, to use a word I heard of in Chaos Theory.

Brian Kennedy 01:10:25 4/30/2004
Hi Everyone
Rob, that is an interesting one to ponder, about Dirk's job.
From his description, I got that the "success" of the job had to do with several factors. I say "success" because I don't think that I would like it, to see it, and would probably not hesitate to change/demo it based on my antipathy for that kind of stark, angular, 90-degree grid-ridden look, in black and white. How it related to the other centers of the house we don't know, but I got the sense that it didn't, very much. It was "structure-preserving" in some sense that was more to do with affection, memory, nostalgia for her father, maybe. And it seemed like there was a strong component of it doing something for her personality structure--she got a thrill of seeing what she wanted materialized at her command, felt "empowered," etc. Henry Shaeffer-Simmern used to say that form forms--that the "maker" gets transformed in the process of creating form. But, her end result was probably pleasing more to herself than anyone else. I doubt it was objectively beautiful in a sensual way. The pleasures of her "hard-edged" kind of an aesthetic seem more mentally based, abstracted away from emotional and tactile dimensions. I thought that was fascinating what Scarpa said, in my quote a while back, about the pure, undecorated cylindrical shape of the microphone being unsatisfactory because it was "UN-finite." The "bad modernists" don't seem to find that a problem, I think their root attitude really springs from a rejection of the body and its inevitable death and a consequent effort to flee to the consolations of immunized mind, where there is the safety of predictability. Dwell Magazine boosts the Modern thing as their main schtick, and they had a chat website and I got on there and "innocently" asked, "What is the difference between "good Modernism" and I gave the example of Gaudi's Modernismo and Bertoia's chair designs for Knoll Company, and "bad Modernism" which I said was the cliche stuff one sees everywhere that is formally banal--for example the endless sofa designs that all use the same stupid-looking steel tube legs. I had just read NOO vol one and I was annoyed that there was no mention of same in their magazine. I provoked a bit of a stir in there, which was fun.
But anyway, the difference between what she did and what the makers of the Ise Shrine did seems pretty great, in that she is clearly a beginner in the game, and something like the Ise Shrine was produced out of a tradition that has lapped her current position on the track thousands of times.
I found CA's case that one can objectively evaluate the beauty of a design by noticing how the centers are handled wonderful as a potential weapon to counterattack the proliferation of "bad" Modern design.

Vasko Drogriski 23:50:20 4/26/2004
Thanks for that.

I guess, what I'm probing for, is more the detailed ongoing 'generative' process of articulating the brief into a'language' for the project, by introducting, implementing and applying the ideas of sequences, centers and patterns.

At the moment I'm toying with the idea of "how do you introduce" the notions of centers, sequences, patterns and then apply them as a process, particularly for remodelling work.

I'm developing a form of contract for myself (partly inspired by the idea of generative building contracts), which is a number of things: contract for services, sequence of steps (as a timeline for the project), cost estimation of project, including cost of my work, skeleton of a 'pattern language' for the project etc... so right from the beggining clients have one document which serves as:
Fee proposal for my services
Generatvie sequence (showing process of design and sequence of steps showing the planning process generally)
Running alongside this are the attached costs with each stage and step.
In this scenario, even if clients don't proceed for some reason, they have a useful document showing them a number of things ie:
costs
design
building
A rough pattern language for the project

This way, I also get paid for everything I do, including quoting and doing the initial talking with clients.
First contact is telephone and then I email them some info. Once they call back its $550 to do first consultation.
It's something I have been wanting feedback on and thanks to Robs work, I'll probably be able to post it there for feedback.

Brians comment on 'reading centers' a while back seemed like an apt clue for me for remodelling work... so I'm trying to include in the above process a number of slightly different approaches in which the 'design' gets tested for its validity. I'm trying to do this by the following:
Statement of the clients needs as a brief, and a starting pattern language from this
Detailed description of the site, pattern language from this
Mapping and reading the centers, pattern language for this (including the latent and living centers)
then I ask what is ideal, or would make the most beatiful place for them, pattern language for this
I can almost overlay the 'pattern languages' in my head, and the client can test it themselves.
With this process, I'm also trying to iron the problem of exorbitant and unnecessary costs of plan modifications for councils and building inspectors... really by keeping the plans very 'generic', give them only whats needed, (the PL and not superflous details about the color of door knobs and toilet roll holers)... if additional technical detail is required this is usually done on site with the inspectors.

I guess I also ask the question as I think it can inform the construction of websites like Rob is doing now. If websites can infact roughly reflect the process of design and building it would be helpful to ordinary people, which not only introduces them to the ideas and approach of CA but gives some instruction how to get things built... taking into account many building, planning and design matters.

About PL.com, if they come to the party, yeah, anything they do with this site is good, otherwise this still 'reflecting' pond might become a stagnant puddle.
I'm wondering also, if they will even provide an active link to Robs site... if not willing to improve it themselves.

dirk visser 14:07:57 4/26/2004
Greetings Vasco,
First casual response to inquiry,trying to establish and ascetain from both sides whether styles and standards will mesh nicely in the context of goal descriptions and initial attempts at description of work to be done style, standards and level of finish etc. I would characterize this phase as, safe, polite, respectful, mutual pshycoanalysis and world view appraisal.
Sometimes things end here in an easy understanding way, from one side or the other or both.
If some level of trust, confidence, comfort, and "sympatico" is established here the next level usually consists of me refering previous work in the form invitation to view, if thats ok or there is previous familiarity, I make a call to estimate the job and do a consultation. I do not charge for this and the is still no pressure on either side to committ untill we wrap up this phase. At that time I give an open quote, either time and material or bid, ballpark figures which I try to make high enough to allow for the inevitable surprises or upgrades.
I have only produced rough sketches generalized material quotes and an hour or two of consultation so far. I'm in it a hundred or two, I guess, but have never charged for this if the job goes South at this point, and you do still lose 'em at this point...
Beyond this point however, I do notify the client that detailed drawings and a real material list is the begining of a committment on their side to initiate compensation.
If that's fine then we get to deeper levels of material and design options, and try to craft the actual working concepts and papers for the job.
I'll line up behind Brian on the 3/3 formula, as the job always does speak back, usually from the get-go.

Brian,
So..There IS accounting for taste?
The contrast thing is a bright light behind a narrow crack, I'll agree.
The enemy of true art, yes that sure fits. It's really what art is up against a lot of the time , huh..?
I just couldn't believe how that little structure broke so many of my personal rules but made her so happy.
I had to acknowledge that the best thing I had done was keep my mouth mostly shut and follow her directions, and execute to the best of my ability. You know: "The Hatchet Man".
Have I recently learned that sometimes this an evolutionary step up from using my experience to sell my own interpretations of the timeless, gracefull, pleasing, forms we as artists and artisans have produced for centuries. That is, if the client ends up genuinely satisfied and happy?
This is unsettling, but it's truth is in my face.
Happy lunch break, guys. Dirk

brian kennedy 11:01:51 4/26/2004
Buenos Dias Dirk
Delightful story from the front, Dirk.
The issue of taste is fascinating.
Wasn't it Picassco who said something like "Good taste is the enemy of Art?" Maybe not, don't remember...anyway...
Some years ago I was selling plants door-to-door in office buildings..yes, I know, NO SOLICITING. It so happened that, at the wholesaler where I got the plants, there were five different designs of little baskets with plastic liners that would hold a 6" or a 10" plastic pot so it did not leak on the floor. So one of these designs I hated, it had a star on it,woven in a different tone of brown bark. So I always picked through and avoided this design. One day they did not have enough of the others so I had to take a star type. Later, out in the field, I had a customer, a Chinese lady. It was often hard to sell to Chinese people, they were very canny and frugal and picky, so I usually did not expect to succeed with them. But this time, she picked a plant, and I showed her the four basket designs that I preferred. She didn't like any of them, and it looked like there would be no deal. Then I remembered that I had the "ugly duckling" star design basket, and I fished it out and showed it to her, She loved it and we had a deal. After that, I started keeping star baskets as part of my offerings, because I realized that for some people, it was a good design.
The satisfaction of your client with the job is great, it's fun. It is cool that you were able to give her the chance to manifest her desire in 4D. (I used to say 3D but after Rob turned me on to that site with the chaos theory links I have become converted.) The emotional "echo-factor" to what her father did was a nice thing to note as one of the factors in what is going on in her feeling core. If they genuinely love it, then there's something fine and true about the incident that seems more important than mere "taste." "Love conquers all."
There's a video called "Thelonius Monk--Straight, No Chaser" in which you can see Monk at a recording session where "for history" they are trying to record in some definitive way a version of one of his classic works. At one point, one of the assisting musicians asks Monk about a particular note, should he play B or B# at this particular point? Monk glances up at him and says "Either one, just play whatever you want." It was like he was saying, when you're in it, you're in it, and a note here or there isn't going to matter.
I think that there is something about the contrast property that REQUIRES bad taste NOO vol 1 page 200. No, maybe not, maybe that is just heresy. It's a good one to kick around though...there is something about contrast that causes alternation in tastes. Like I noticed someone said about the last year's International Furniture Fair "The winds of Modernism blow ever more sterile." And in design magazines I start to see a move toward frivolity, humor, anthropomorphism,---anything to try to do something else besides try to invent one more way to pare things down further, to subtract something, which has been the darling approach for quite a while now.


brian kennedy

brian kennedy 09:55:04 4/26/2004
Vasco,
I work by drawing and dreaming(awake) until I have ideas that satisfy the requirements as interestingly(beautifully, elegantly, surprisingly, inventively, delightfully) as possible. Then I try to figure out how to manipulate the materials to get them to do what I have drawn. Then I sell the client on it from drawings, or if they already trust me, I show them the drawings as a way of showing them what I am trying to do. When I actually start to work with the material, it enters the dialogue too and shows me things that I had not been able to anticipate that increase the interest, so I make use of such discoveries on the way. When finished, I like to say that my work is the result of a 3-way collaboration: 1/3 me, 1/3 the client, and 1/3 the material.

brian kennedy

Dirk Visser 01:50:27 4/26/2004
Hola Brian,
Absolutely you do gotta love 'em!
Like my youngest wife used to tell me when she was turning on the charm, " Sweetheart, it must be so hard for you living in this world.." right ,right, dream on...but girls do have ways of making you believe something that silly don't they?

On to wieghtier subjects;
Last week I built a little shade structure along the back of a house out of (2)2x6beams, (6) 4x4 posts and (~80)2x2 laths. The 4 foot long lath was butted between the beams on 3" centers giving a "cieling" of 16 feet of the 20 foot total. Then we covered the lathed section with white corrugated fiberglass, with only about 2 inches of drip edge reveal, past the 2x6 main beam. No 2x2 lath cross pieces show unless you are close to getting under it. All the wood was prepainted carbon black before assembly.

Goofy?, You bet! I wouldn't have designed or wanted to build that thing in a million years. It was not "me", and never would be.

Let me try to say this carefully...
The client was extremely pleased with the effect of her design and the execution of the construction.
She told me how it supported aspects of her minimalist, "edgy" design philosopy.
I heard from the housekeeper that it had similarities to the old pipeframe shade the client's deceased father had rigged up many years ago when my client was a little girl in this same house.
I began to feel proud of my work in a way that I hadn't realized was possible. Nothing I could have dreamed up, beautiful and balanced, classic as it might be, could ever get close to the "rightness" and emotional power of this offbeat little creation for my client in this place at this time.

Am I only now, at this late stage, learning this? How could I have been so opinionated and pompous all those years?

These twists just keep coming don't they?
Peace, compadres, Dirk

brian kennedy 11:56:02 4/25/2004
To Rob and Matt
You can see an interesting critique of computers as a design tool by Milton Glaser at http://www.icograda.org/web/feature-past-single.shtml?pfl=feature-single-2.param&op2.rf1=56

Glaser believes that digital design suffers from what he calls a "lack of fuzzyness" which tends to resolve ambiguities too quickly. He feels that there is a dialectic process of ideas back and forth between the designer and the drawing within the fuzzy period that is helpful in arriving at a really good idea, and that computers interfere with this. Something to bear in mind while designing virtual-reality design tools so that you can invent a solution to this matter.

brian kennedy

brian kennedy 11:26:22 4/25/2004
Hi all:
Dirk, about the motivation to build a custom dwelling from scratch:
Tadao Ando, a self-taught Pritzker-prize-winning architect, was asked what motivated him to do architecture, and he replied "Hatred." He was so dissatisfied with the built world that he was moved to build something else.
I feel a somewhat related motivation, part frustration at what exists, and part pleasure of making things that satisfy and explore my own sense of rightness.

Regarding building inspectors and issues of power, and control of safety, I can offer two stories. One is about Frank Lloyd Wright. When he was working on the Johnson and Johnson Building, his design had some golf-tee-shaped columns that each needed to carry, say, ten tons of load. The inspectors disputed his design and demanded a full-scale test. So a column was built and the test began, but FLW stood right under it as he waved the crane to begin loading the column! When the load reached ten tons, he kept waving for more weight until it approached twenty tons. Each time more weight was put on he tapped the column with his cane, sensing it. Finally he stepped away, but had it loaded more until it collapsed.
Gaudi had a run-in with inspectors when he had huge columns like elephant's feet protruding onto the sidewalk. As he was adament that there were no straight lines in nature he refused to be dictated to by the sidewalk line, and he just built it over all official protest. Those geniuses...ya just gotta love 'em!

brian kennedy

Hey Vasko
Sorry, no more info on United Stand, I looked a little bit a couple of years back, but didn't turn up anything. I will let you know if I run across anything.
Have not read that Odum book or seen it. I read one book once by an ecologist named Odum who was studying ecosystems in terms of the calories of energy in the sunlight that fell on the land and what the plant and animal populations on that land managed to capture and use. He also showed what the energy costs were to make a solar collector vs the yield of energy from the device and I was disappointed because he seemed to show that technology was not necessarily going to be a panicea for the world. (I had been taught to believe technology was going to make everything right when I used to watch DISNEYLAND as a child.)
I suggest putting a request to abebooks.com. They will find it used and connect you to a bookstore that has it, and if you like the price they send it to you.
About multigeneration house sharing, it's done all the time here too, same story --ethnic people co-operating with each other as a family unit. Usually in poor neighborhoods where there is more of an opportunity to break a few rules--the neighbors aren't going to call the cops because they are selling crack, or whatever. Or to fill large apartment houses managers look the other way while Mexicans share an apartment ten ways. I myself confess to, at the peak, sharing a 2 bedroom apartment in Berkeley with, I think 17 people. We all used to skinnydip in the pool. We just had fun like little kids staying over at their friends' house. We participated in a "food conspiracy," group shopping for forty households, divvying it all up and distributing it to each other.
I read your long past message to Rosemead? Farm on here, which sounded like a well-planned village idea somewhere in Australia. I was impressed by the practical ides for sharing the land, and that there was actually a govt program that encouraged and allowed alternate approaches to how to live together and found a village. I met with a group of county officials about a 27 acre property I am in process of trying to buy. I showed the planner a xeroxed cohousing scheme pasted on the land map and said, I want to do something like this. How do I do it? What language do I have to use? What hoops do I have to jump thru? And she was young enough and smart enough not only to recognize and name the illustration from the book, but to tell me I could do it and tell me the language I needed to express the idea in. Whereas two days before I had talked to a different official, a little older and a lot dumber, and he completely stonewalled me, acted like there was no way to do it within their system at all. So then I took my plan to a hearing with reps from fish and game, water, planning, building, x, y, z--about ten of them in all. I said I want to have a bath house here a theater here, a meditation hall here, a kitchen here, a laundry here, ponds here , here, and here, ceramics, wood, metal, jewelry, weaving studios here, here, here, and fifty teepees located whereever the individuals feel like setting up that dayweekyear. And they as much told me that if I was willing to pay a few thousand to get reports by botanists, archeologists etc., I could maybe do some of what I wanted...so it wasn't completely impossible legally...

Vask Drogriski 19:10:44 4/24/2004
Brian,

Do you have any other information on 'United Stand' and the owner builder clause?

Fossil fuel dependency is surely a strong dynamic for change... once it was the 'long term and global effects' of fossil fuel use, but from observation of peoples reactions/actions it seems that the local and immediate effects are equally powerful and the primary drivers for change now... particularly as the local effects (environmental commonly) have effects on health. A few years ago, migrant communities here began hunting kangaroo, mostly after being told by their doctors, and increasingly naturopaths, that they should eat healthy, organic, non processed foods and meats especially. Eating native meats, and foods generally, is no new idea now, but it has been an ideological and ecological position which has been promoted by sections of the 'ecological movement'... ordinary people were not discussing the ecological benefits much, they were just taking their families out and hunting kangaroos cause they wanted to live longer, primarily.
I agree that things do change...
Have any of you been able to get your hands on Howard Odums 'Prosperous way down' in the States... its unavailable here... but I have heard it is a worthwile read.

And about housing, you must have something similar in the US... community... over the years there have been many initiatives here in Melbourne where people join up in small groups, put their finances together, or groups with different levels of finance who have some mutual benefits from coming together and this way they purchase what they can not afford on their own... this gives people a foot in the economy, or housing market at least. But this is nothing new, migrants have been doing this for a long time, Vietnamese families, extended families, sometimes 4 or 5 families are commonly seen living together... they pay off the house, transform their collective labour into equity, give it over to the first family move on to the next and etc... they own and have created some of the liveliest inner city suburbs in Melbourne.
I have always been intrigued by how many centers, condensed and overlapping patterns there must be in situations such as these... 4 or 5 families in a modest sized house!

Brian Kennedy 21:03:01 4/23/2004
Matt
I agree with Rob's comments in support of Christopher Alexander's ideas about how to adjust who holds the power in building decisions. CA goes into it in some detail in NOO vol 2, where he describes architects directing tradesmen/craftsmen/artisans in return for an overseeing fee. He thinks if such arrangements work out for all concerned that they will become increasingly used and proliferate. That's how a lot of great cathedrals were probably built. Geoffrey Bawa did some pretty cool stuff--page 141 NOO VOL 2 shows a very cool door by a sculptor in one of Bawa's houses.
I myself have not had good experiences collaborating with architects or landscape architects or interior designers because I find that they tend to be threatened by my work. For instance I was once called upon to design a firescreen by an architect for their client. When I showed a drawing of my idea to the architect, who was a straight-line, square kind of a guy, he said, "I'm afraid this isn't the kind of thing I had in mind." I said OK and figured, forget it, I can't work within his limits. So, I didn't attempt to send any more drawings. But, he made the mistake of showing the drawing to the customer, who LOVED it and wanted it as the centerpiece of the living room. So I ended up making it and two other things for the house. She just called back, three years later, and told me it's the best thing in her house and now wants me to do a sculpture for the outside garden. So, in that case I escaped with the job, but I have found that if you do too good a job, the establishment is threatened and will try to cut you out.

During the tenure of Jerry Brown as Gov of California, an interesting thing happened. There were a lot of "freaks" who were living in Mendocino and Humbolt Counties who had gone into the woods and built artistic houses, as in the photos in the book, The Woodbutcher's Art. The county officials started prowling around out there and "red-tagging" (condemning) the houses for code violations. The people got together and formed an organization United Stand which was ultimately successful in getting "Section K" the owner-builder clause inserted in the local codes which said you are free to build as you want to, you just can't sell it to somebody else. I don't know the current status of this matter. It was an enlightened time--Sym Van der Ryn (one of the founding organizers of the first Earth Day) was State Architect and Gary Snyder, the poet, was State Arts Commissioner. I don't know about the current status of this issue. Some people I know just go ahead and build and ask forgivenss later, if ever. But you need enough land to have some invisibility. In some Mendocino neighborhoods, when people see inspectors, they let their dogs out and call the neighbors who do the same.

Brian Kennedy

Rob Asumendi 15:22:55 4/23/2004
Matt,

I've been fiddling with the idea of an unfolding-friendly CAD app for a while now. I created a prototype at www.asumendi.com/garden/ where you can walk around a 3d environment and build a garden just by clicking the mouse. (If you check it out, you need a ghz or better processor for smooth movement... the asdw keys move you around).

A version for buildings, I just wonder if being able to create the drawing is the most important thing -- Mr. Alexander says that he's built many buildings where they unfolded during the design process, but then the measurements were transferred to drawings, and the building built from the drawings -- the result of which was the death of the building. In NOO, I think he mentions something like 'even if you could construct a flower atom-by-atom, would it be alive?' Of course, our intuition is no, it would not.

I'd rather design something that helped people work with these generative ideas, and then have more and more people demand a change in the logistics of building. If you try to work unfolding into the current system, I don't know, seems like a lot of work to create the tool for a questionable end result. So I'm kind of thinking something that starts with a panoramic view of the site, and you can place the natural features, place the stakes for the outline of the house, then start 'sketching' details in 3d space (like what they're doing in proce55ing.net) Get people to realize that this is better, that yeah, maybe they'd rather have a beautiful place for their kids to play or outdoor rooms, as opposed to Corian counters and the oak cabinets.

I guess the question is, if someone is already committed to building a house from scratch, a custom design (i.e. 0.001 of urban and sub-urban Americans at least) then why compromise at this stage in the game? I see that the goal is to allow more people to take part in the process, by making it more affordable, but I don't know. Do most people even have a choice of what their house looks like anymore? Coming from Chicago, it was just 1)builder buys chunk of land 2)builder tells people what they're building 3) people rush to get that house because it's a little further away from the congested madness than anything else. What incentive does a builder have for changing this arrangement? Does the owner of a large development company care whether his employees enjoy their jobs? Does he speak Spanish?

In other words, how do we get the land back from the developers? (In Chicago, our rich clients would pay $400,000 for an old house and knock it down for the lot... I will not be able to afford a $400k lot.) Even here in Bellingham, a city of about 65,000, this is happening, and I imagine this is one of the few towns where there's still some living structure, and people are even motivated to change the system to keep it. I guess I wonder if the cost of *producing* drawings is what's preventing generative architecture -- or is the real cost in the implications of building from a drawing?

Rob Asumendi

Matt Titus 03:29:35 4/23/2004
Hello all!

I'm a friend of Dirk's. I'm interested in architecture, but I work as a software engineer. Thanks to Silicon Valley bubble-era title inflation, I once had business cards that claimed me to be a 'product architect', and though this was clearly an example of an employer pandering to the egos of their engineers, I can see that there are many parallels between software design and architecture.

Rob, I really like your site. It's good to see someone using new tools in useful ways.

Someone mentioned _The_Production_Of_Houses_ the other day, and I recalled the difficulties Alexander encountered with the building inspector and city planner/architect people on that project. The building inspector et. al. wanted to see complete 'architectural' drawings of the houses, and objected to the materials and construction techniques that Alexander was using. He seemed pretty upset that they were giving him such a hard time. One thing in particular that stuck with me was his complaint about the drawings. He was doing some of the design of the houses as he went, and said that it would bankrupt the project if he had to have detailed plans drawn for each unique house, and have the plans redrawn each time he modified the house. A while back Dirk observed that he used two ways of creating things - a cut-and-fit approach for some things, and a plastic arts approach for things that could be molded or sculpted. One of the really remarkable things about Alexander's approach was that he blended the two - he was almost sculpting the houses as he went, and used cut-and-fit only where necessary. That made the planners and inspectors uncomfortable, as they were used to buildings being completely planned before ground was broken. The other problem they had was with Alexander's unconventional construction materials and techniques, both of which (incidentally) were very much plastic arts - light concrete vaults supported initially by lathwork and canvas, and building blocks cast out of local soil.

I got to wondering how these sort of problems with the establishment could be overcome without incurring the exhorbitant cost of many changes to drawn plans. First, assume that the building establishment is not going to change, or will only change slowly and reluctantly, so someone who wants to build must accomodate them. Computer modelling of the houses and site could help; certainly there's nothing more plastic than bits. Cost would be kept to a minimum if the modelling software could produce plans that were acceptable to building inspectors and their ilk, though one would have to be careful how often one called the inspector to validate a new set of plans!

The problem with unconventional building materials and techniques, would be solved if the materials and techniques became conventional. Structural features could be designed by 'real architects' and validated by engineers. So if you want a light concrete vault above a room, pick and place it into your virtual plan. The software will tell you if the distance you are trying to span with the vault is too great. When you have completed a part of the plan, have the program produce a 3D model of it from any viewpoint you like.

While this is a far cry from the sculpted houses CA made in Mexico, tools like these could put the power to design buildings into the hands of ordinary folks, which would be a welcome change from having to consume an identical house in a vast bland tract. If you've made it this far, well, I surely value your comments!

Ciao,

Matt Titus From Niles, California

Rob Asumendi 16:03:04 4/22/2004
The registration page is up on my notebook tool - http://dhnotebook.asumendi.com

You can sign up for an account, create a notebook and start filling it with centers and describing them. Currently, the only things that don't work yet are a) moving/deleting a center b)deactivating a center.

I've divided centers into three categories, based on the kinds of questions Mr. Alexander asks about them: land centers (prompts you with questions like 'relationship to the street/neighbors', place centers (the basic center type), and object centers (things you can't really 'be in'). You can change a center's category whenever you want.

All you can do on the menu is a) login/signup b) logout/view your workspace - the forums, articles, etc. are nonfunctional... I'm just testing the notebook program right now. Any links that take you back to the main page, I just haven't made the articles/whatever yet.

Things that are currently being added to the notebook tool: a sketch tool for adding sketches to centers. Creating your own database of family/friends/visitors so each person's role can be described in each center (if desired).

Again, you should be able to view my notebook http://dhnotebook.asumendi.com/notebook/view/44 as a reference. I'm really enjoying the "transitions" aspect, having the software prompt me to describe the relationship between a room and a tree you see through the window.

Anyhow, have fun - any feedback is appreciated!

Rob Asumendi

brian kennedy 08:41:50 4/22/2004
Dirk,

brian kennedy 23:30:40 4/21/2004
Hi Rob and Dirk,
I'm still looking thru the Buddhabrot Images, wishing I had a large wall size one of one of the black and white treatments. That has the same graphic charm for me as those moth wings I enjoyed so much. I get distracted by the Strange Attracter force in them, which I somehow feel personally attracted to. The delightful, fascinating quality of predictable appearances of the Unpredictable--Shiva dancing, is something I recognize again from my interplay with Adi Da Samraj. He has used the phrase "always new" as a description of the Divine, it resonates with me. The fact that this apparently innocuous-looking equation blows into that form of an iconic "Buddha" is amazing. When I first saw it I thought it was some graphics program making interesting modifications of an existing line drawing of a Buddist icon, and I thought it was great then, but the truth is much better!
I have described the effect I try to produce in my work as a kind of "controlled chaos." My interest in "roughness" comes out of being attracted to the free, non-rote quality of a pattern that is visibly fluxing as it moves, dances. "It don't mean a thing if it ain't got that swing." (quote: Cab Calloway?) I was amazed to read about how the 4th Dimension has exactly that quality of little glitches-"errors"-pulses that throw off its predictability, preventing boredom if you really see what is happening. This is what attracted me aesthetically about the quilts I described in Eli Leon's book, etc. for instance, and in Gaudi's work and Jujol's work. And I might as well add Miro to the list. Brian Kennedy 03:12:30 4/21/2004
Hey Rob, It's the wee hours, I just got home and checked what's been happening and WOW this Buddhabrot is beautiful!!! Dirk,
Regarding discovery/invention, I notice that the word invention seems to correspond to Latin roots for "in" and "wind" as in "ventilation." And it seems a pretty good beginning to a subjective attempt to describe the experience of participating in an "invention-event"--it just blows into your head. Often people describe being "receivers" of "creative information" in a very passive way when it actually "comes,"--dreams, trances, absorbtive reveries--even though they may have much more strenuously "set the stage" by making many kinds of preparations--consciously studying something, imitating masters, playing scales, etc. Edison comes to mind as someone who really busted ass on exploratory investigations as part of the process, whereas Edgar Cayce would just lie down and enter a sleep-like trance state. For almost thirty years now, I have been a student of Adi Da Samraj. He often will pose a question that acts as a koan (to use the Buddhist term) such as, "What do you do, exactly, when you want to have a thought?" to point out that many times we assume that we are the active agent in situations, but actually it's not really the case. We aren't the agent that is doing the breathing or the digesting, for instance, even though our language is set up in such a way that we beg the question. We say,"Oh, well that digestion is being handled by my parasympathetic nervous system--it's all kind of automatic but it's me you know--'I'm' the CEO here." When actually it is more accurate to say we are being lived. And, we don't know what a single thing is. We can collect all kinds of descriptive information ABOUT things, but we can't account at all for the Isness of anything, the Beingness. Even though Adi Da has said "All there is is is," we don't know what "is" is.

Brian Kennedy

Rob Asumendi 18:29:26 4/20/2004
Brian,

Computers can be useful for that kind of thing (the moth-wing pictures) up to a point. Sometimes you can get lucky and program something simple that mimics complex natural phenomena, and sometimes you have to know much more about the phenomena. But exponential growth algorithms can be found in abundance.... There has been an explosion of such "experiments" in the last couple years with the release of Macromedia Flash, which is a very friendly environment for anyone to learn to program in. You can find lots of interesting approximations of natural phenomena at "flash experiment" sites like www.levitated.net, www.bit-101.com, and the sites they link to. There's a great variety to be found at these sites because Flash is so flexible, but there are some really beautiful things there. The "Buddhabrot" at levitated.net is a recent experiment that's particularly mesmerizing. If I saw some pictures of the wings, I might be able to find something specific.

brian kennedy 13:09:25 4/18/2004
Hey Rob
That is wonderful that you might facilitate image exchange and drawing and hyperlinking to overcome the limits of tree-type formations.
One use I am interested in finding out how to use a computer for has to do with studying/replicating patterns on moth wings. Around here we have, in particular, some "Oak Moths" who have very complex, very fine patterns on their wings that seem to help them blend in with Oak Tree bark. Some years back I was reading with a lamp in a camp during the active season of the moths ,so they kept landing on my reading materials. Now, the colors they are using are not very attention grabbing--lots of greys and browns--so I had never really apppreciated them before. HOWEVER, when I got a really intimate look at ten or twenty, I saw that each one was different, like snowflakes, and their patterns were mysteriously, utterly spectacular, even though and maybe even BECAUSE OF the fact that they were using such an apparently limited palette. To try to explain: boundaries and gradients were amazingly utilized to produce design lines that were often extremely subtle and yet extremely sharp at the same time. Let's say there were one million grey tones. Sometimes a pattern would be formed by grey #597,652 outlining a field of grey #597,653. To form such a design by human efforts would, I imagine, take years and years just to make all the marks, not to mention deciding where to put them. With computer technology I think one could get a handle on first photograghing them and then scanning them, then replicating them and studying them to understand their appeal better and then hopefully using them in skillful ways.
One of the fascinating aspects of these wings is that although the designs seem infinitely varied and "wild" they also seem completely right, calm, rested! They all seem perfectly related to the shapes of the wings, which are very elegant and poised shapes, and their (the wing shapes') bilaterally symmetrical effect acts as counterpose to the apparent chaos of the patterns inside the wing field. Chaos is not the right word, there is profound freedom and profound order evident at the same time.It's the symmetry/asymmetry riddle of the Japanese kimono again. That is something I would really like to get a better understanding of. If you can point me to presently available computer tools to bring to bear, I would love it.
The next thing about moth/butterfly wings is the color lesson. I love looking at the things that they pull off, totally wild and yet with perfect poise. CA worried about his color choices on the Upham House turning out to be "silly." However, interesting color was necessary to enliven the grey mass, and the amount of color was so small that any vividness was not in danger of being too much, imo. I would have gone for color delineating the boundary of the edge of the roof as well, but under-coloring is one of my compaints about houses generally in this neck of the woods.

brian kennedy 00:04:28 4/16/2004
here's somebody interesting--Carlos Scarpa. From: Carlos Scarpa Architect, Intervening with History. Remodeling by a master artist/architect of classical old Italian buildings at a very high level of sensitivity.
"I think that Venice, more than any other Italian city, could accomodate the modern expression of architecture. Because of certain of it's asymmetries--its very varied skyline, with high and low buildings, its streets broad and narrow. And it has very beautiful interior spaces, so if it wold be possible to preserve things of this type in the historic fabric, there would be no fear of spoiling the city, so long as designs were carried out in a manner...worthy of it." Carlos Scarpa 1978

Museo di Castelvecchio
" At Castelvecchio the builders of olden times had tackled the problem of the formal identity of a series of dwellings and their connection. The rooms were laid out in rows, beween two walls distant and distinct from each other. They were the two facades of the castle. The more ancient of the two walls is solid, almost without apertures. It opened towards the river, on the outside. The other,more recent one was less massive and opened out onto the garden. You see how the building retains its identity in time: it's a basic principle....I wanted to preserve the originality, the character of every room, but I didn't want to use the wooden beams of the earlier restoration. Since the rooms were square, I set a paired steel beam to support the point where the two reinforced concrete beams crossed, so indicating the main lines of the building's formal structure. Where they crossed the importance of that square was emphasized because the crossing of the two beams in the center implies the pillar which helps define the square.
This is the visual logic I wanted to use as a frame of reference. The way the beams were made also brings out the visual logic, but only in the details. I could have used the steel profiles already on the market, but it would have been an engineering solution without any visual emphasis. I thought it would be more interesting to take the profiles and make a composition out of them. The new joints reveal the structure of the element and the new functions." p67

"Scarpa called for a return to the "ancient " discipline of drawing as the generative force of invention, and with it the recovery of the poetic independence of design, with a "distictive personal character that would lend each work the moral authority that everyone in the world of art must strive for." Beginning with a famous lecture in 1963, he strenuously defended decoration, arguing--with the example of the microphone stem's tubular column before him--that forms remain 'un-finite,' and thus inexpresssive, without it. He believed that a syntax of color and ornament becomes inevitable in an architectural project from the moment the designer makes the first choice of a material, resist as one might.
Scarpa also advocated a return to the sensory delight that comes from what can be touched and felt in each material, to the intellectual excitment that derives from exploring and unveiling its structural characteristics,and to the logical pleasures of seeing how distinctive materials with different properties can be wed. He asked us to re-acknowledge the potential of materials to drive the generative process and produce a ruling idea for the larger work:"It is a curious facultythis, which enables us to sense that a certain dimensional factor, a thickness for instance, can become the determining characteristic of the physical value of things." Above all, he insisted that the building of a vocabulary of material and ornament was a critical step in capturing those senses---tactile, psychological, and mnemonic, that open a work of architecture to "maximum expression and meaning." p13

A remodelers remodeler--Joe Bob sez "Check it out!"

brian kennedy 22:50:55 4/15/2004
Dirk and Vasco
A few quotes from Rex Roberts, Your Engineered Home published 1964.
"Some years ago in West Groton, Dick Bissel was putting up a house. I stopped to look the place over. Dick, a friend and fellow carpenter, rested while I looked.
After a while, when I hadn't managed to find anything very flattering to say, Dick remarked, "You have to admit it's too good for chickens." I admitted it. He frowned. "But it's not quite big enough for cows."
In criticising his own handiwork, Dick expressed his sorrow that most human houses are less satisfactory than they might have been. Somehow, in this largest of human expenditures, we are not getting our money's worth." p. 10

"Discussion of remodeling must run either to a few hundred words and stop there, or go on to several million. There is no sensible place to stop in between.
Our few hundred words here will be devoted to stating the general case against remodeling. If you, for excellent reasons of your own, decide to ignore this generalization and plunge ahead with a remodeling job, the number of problem variations multiplied by the number of possible solutions becomes astronomical. In remodeling, sight-unseen advice is worthless. A million words wouldn't even get us started.
By remodeling I do not mean normal maintainance or even normal updating to keep a house from becoming obsolete. I refer to major structural changes and major technical additions which are neede to make a building too good for chickens." p168
"I grant you that the very difficulties of remodeling can stimulate the imagination, sometimes producing an end result of great charm. If your imagination is like that, remaining silent in the empty outdoors but triggered by an assembly of roughly rectangular walls and rooofs, a tumble-down barn is what you need, cheap. If these are your symptoms, my suggestion is that you buy the barn, stare at it for a while,draw your plans, then tear down the barn and start fresh somewhere else. I have already admitted that great charm can result from a major remodeling. Unfortunately, charm created in this manner is in most cases an expensive luxury." "We all know, however, that all generalizations are false, including this one. Under what circumstances might it me emotionally if not financially sound to undertake a remodeling?
"In the preservation, of course, of a minor historical monument. The house becomes adinnertable conversation piece which can repay all the effort you put into it....In the use of enclosed space, the shell of which is in good repair. I'm a sucker for this one myself, because it keeps the carpenters in out of the rain..." p. 168

Rex Roberts was an engineer, carpenter, musician who really had some great creative ideas about house-building. He claims to be able to help you make a superior custom house for yourself for roughly half of what you would pay for the same size mass-market product.

D. Visser 14:41:57 4/15/2004
Dear B. & V.
Mulling over your foray into repair- renovate-remodel, I was making a tricky cut for some lintel trim clearance, getting some old siding out of the way, when something possibly helpful occured to me.
First, there is the almost medical, surgical aspect.(Don't cut too deep or far.)
Second encompasses the "can of worms syndrome" of finding soft wood, outdated utility infrastructure, minimal insulation, foundation issues, etc.
Third, How does this change Tie In structurally?, esthetically?, investmentwise?
Fourth, how are human activities and uses, privacy, views etc. affected in the old and new sections of the work?

How many times have you heard people say "It's way easier to build new.", or " In hindsight we should have started over from scratch."?

Successfully done, there's few things more satisfying than a complementary addition or renovation, but the above oft-heard phrases are a testimony to the difficulty of pulling it off easily in the modern scenario.

I think the primary reason it is harder to renovate or remodel is that the old work must be constantly read for "centers" and "wholeness". And, the old work, or it's outlying border elements, are often in your way. Even out at the edge of a new addition there are visual, auditory, and other physical and psychic connections to the antecedent structure that will be made, harmoniously or not.
New centers must be established, old centers must be preserved, supported, re-interpreted. It gets to be a bit of a puzzle compared to making the thing up out of whole cloth....

Catch you later, Dirk

P.S. Thanks, Ih Tiao, for the link. An unexpected view!

D. Visser

D. Visser 13:44:17 4/14/2004
Delving a little more into the Marsh Arab ecology I agree that "remnants" of human experience, and physical genetic/mineral material, are preliminary keys to getting the thing going again.
It's a good bet the slate has some of Picasso's last drawing in "pintamento".

Nature of Order vol. 2 chapter 1, "The Principle of Least Action" explains river behavior which creates the marshes. A dike breaks across all this balanced structure, symmetry, and "laziness". It is a catastrophic change.
How far do we want to go back in our "constructive demolition"? what was the pre-Saddam hydrology? When did the desire for fans and freezers start? It's hard to keep 'em down on the farm after they've seen Gay Paree!
On the other hand, you don't know what you got till it's gone and there may be many who are willing to devote there lives to getting it back, if given the chance.
Reading further in chapter 2 we come across another related universal with great explanatory power called "minimum symmetry breaking".
"The system acts to preserve as much of it's structure as possible" is the root quote.
It should be recognized that our human motivations are also gathered into the fold here. We are acting as a part of nature, trying to preserve our essential physical and cultural structures whether they be human bodies, families, governments, religions, neighborhoods, etc. Sadamm saw the area as out of his reach and threatening so he dried it up to protect his "structure".
When you have creative power and free will, you get this kind of collision of worlds. One system imposes on another, often needlessly, due to fear and insecurity.
These debilitating emotions are ultimately sourced in the irony of the genetic survival game which built us, but which our creative and destructive powers have now outstripped. Another case of a phase shift struggling to transform, while preserving as much of it's structure as possible.
I actually think Alexander is onto something with huge expanatory and unifying power, and hope, for a species which can think.
He's come a long way from "The Quality Without a Name".

Next time, d.v.

brian kennedy 16:34:02 4/13/2004
Hi Dirk
Enjoyed your definitions and your joke about my joke.

A further addition to our (including Vasco) consideration about remodeling/demolition, which even though it started as a curiosity offers a chance to cite an interesting thing:
I was looking through a paperback full of quotes by Picasso about art to various people over many years. It was obvious that he didn't care about contradicting himself at all, that he enjoyed being a trickster with his verbal communications. One thing he said that particularly struck me was a pronouncement that "painting is destruction." I thought he must be pulling the leg of whoever he was talking to. About a year later I happened to see a film that was made showing Picasso painting. He painted on plexiglass for the process. It was very interesting because a very rapid recording of sequences of a master. He finished "a painting," and then rubbed out about 90% of it and generated another painting off of the fragment. Then he did it again and again about 15 times. At a certain point, the filmmaker said, I only have 2 minutes of film left, is that OK? (Picasso had just wiped out almost everything again) Picasso is completely at ease, says "sure" takes a drag on his cigarette, and finishes another really outrageous composition within the remaining time without any apparent effort. I plan to re-watch that one to study it in the light of CA's 15 transformations/sequences. But it was interesting what he demonstrated about "destruction" too. Turned out he was not just playing with the interviewer when he said it. You get to see him make and wipe out 10 or 20 million bucks worth of art in about a half hour.
Jujol also was known for ordering an iron railing, then, when it was delivered, deliberatly having it bent and broken and deformed before installing it, and his work is perhaps the most alive I have seen. "Punctuated equilibrium" applied, perhaps. Another story was that while he was instructing the smith to twist and deform the iron, sometimes it would break, and Jujol would scold the iron. For him, building materials were alive.
I knew a guy from Nigeria who had been raised in an African magical context as a child but had come to the US for a degree in engineering. One day he was telling me about a rock masonry project he was doing a beautiful job on, and he mentioned that the rocks were very angry about being moved from their resting places and put into the wall, and that if you were not careful while working with them, they would look for a chance to drop on your foot and nail you if they had a chance.

Vasco,

You are probably the first and slightly ahead of you time in making the connection, but I believe there is great relavance and utility in the application with much more to follow in time.
Alexander may have begun with architecture, but the implications of his present work are certainly powerful enough to lend some clarity to your question of a nature, culture, political, intersection in the former marshlands of the Tigris-Euphraties delta.

I'll give it a very quick stab and revisit later after a hoped-for post by Brian.
I think one important feature is critical mass. Whatever re-settlement is initiated should be large enough in natural and social size to endure as a sustasinable "strong center". Yet it should have area to grow into, which it can prepare as "future latent centers", with solutions the community discovers.
Chances are there will be fans and freezers, if people want them now.
It's likely the "Old Ways" will only be partially reclaimed after this catastrophic episode.
The seedbed for new ideas should include restoring and maintaining the character of the marshes, as much as possible so that non-generic lives for the settlers are at least a portion of the scene, otherwise what's the point? Might as well dike and pave it.
Also the people, their motivational, intellectual, and physical resources, are crucial. This is probably the answer to your "what would you do first?" query. Find a few core groups who have had the marsh experience and have the drive to make it a life's work again, then empower them to make truly important decisions about what happens next. Mistakes will be made, sometimes expensive, That's part of it. This is the cost. As long as things move forward and the whole continues to transform and move outward, keeping the essence of the "marsh pattern", you will have life generating sequences operating.
If down the road, education and tourism become factors, it will be an affirmation of success, along with whatever alarms are raised.

brian kennedy 14:56:54 4/13/2004
Hi Vasco

Have you ever heard of Cabeza De Vaca? He was a would-be conquistador who washed up on the shore of Florida hoping to duplicate Pizarro's big successes with Montezuma down in Mexico. But when he and his crew hit the beach it was reverse D-Day and the Indians wiped nearly all of them out. Cabeza did some great nude getaway running and avoided getting killed, but found himself walking friendless and alone out in the boonies with nothing to eat, wear, or defend himself with. He ended up getting very skinny out there, and then he was captured by some other Indians, who could not decide if he was a human being or a devil or a god. So, the chief, who happened to have a sick daughter at the time, decided that if Cabeza De Vaca could heal his daughter overnight, then he must be some kind of good spirit, and if not, they would kill him in the morning. They communicated this somehow to Cabeza and put him in the tent with her. He proceeded to pray his ass off, and during the night sometime, he basically had a catastrophic phase shift (very burnt-up ex-conquistador+purificatory ordeal of fasting and wandering+extreme life-or-death pressure=sho'nuff, real-deal healing shaman) and awakened into a capacity to feel and channel a healing Force. In the morning, everybody was all smiles! The Indians passed him on to the next tribe and over the next few years he walked across the continent, healing the sick. When he got over to California, which was Spanish Territory at the time, he got on a boat for Spain and wrote a report to the King/Queen detailing his experiences. He also discovered that after he got back to "civilization" and got comfortable again, his powers faded away. I mention this because my feeling is that human beings have spiritual possibilities under duress that they usually can't engage because they prefer to be comfortable, and comfort seems to make these abilities recede. There is a connection between "tapas" (no, not Spanish snacks--I mean it in the yogic sense of the word for austerities!)and "siddhis" (abilities) that has been exploited since ancient time by seekers who wanted to move beyond the limits of the "normal." So, my feeling is that when situations arise such as in Iraq, where there are double-binds to the Catch-22nd power everywhere you look, you got a problem that you need a heavy-duty Spiritual Realizer to intervene in.

As far as more ordinary people who have a history of effective discoveries that could relate to a situation like Iraq, I would suggest consulting Masanobu Fukuoka, if he is still alive. He is/was a remarkable man who wrote One Straw Revolution, The Natural Way to Farm and a third book which I forget. He was the inspiration behind Bill Mollison's work, The Permaculture guy. Fukuoka was a scientist who had a catastrophic phase shift incident as a post doctoral researcher in Japan in the twenties. One day he went into a feverish delirium in which he had two realizations which he expressed as "Human Knowledge amounts to nothing" and "plants will grow by themselves."
On the basis of this insight he quit his job cold ( the conservative Japanese colloqial expression for this
act at the time was "cutting your own throat" and returned to his father's Mandarin Orange orchard, built a hut, and started to take care of trees. The first thing that happened was, he refused to prune ("Plants will grow by themselves") and lost 400 trees. He later found that pruning threw off the natural sequence of branching and once you started, you had to continue. So trees he grew from scratch he never pruned. He went on to develop what he called "Do Nothing Farming" and performed feats like dry farming rice, no rice paddy, no weeding, no insecticides, no fertilizer, but yields higher than conventional yields in standard agriculture. In his later years he was interested in working on how to reverse the process of desertification. He advocated asking the Earth itself how to stop it. He proposed initiating a dialog with the Earth by taking all our ICBMs, packing the warheads full of every kind of seed, and shooting them out into the Sahara to see what would grow. He was a very radical, outspoken critic of the same kind of world view that CA is pointing to the limits of, and he had several great discoveries to show for his methods. Genius of his caliber I think is probably available in our population to help with messes on that kind of scale, if they can have a chance to be heard and co-operated with.
Fukuoka was shut down by the Japanese government after he refused to let them control a superior strain of rice he developed to ameliorate third world hunger. Par for the course in the Kali Yuga.

The place is the Arab marsh lands in Iraq which as you both may be aware were drained by Hussein some time ago in attempt to destroy dissident comunities, rebels, bandits, draft dodgers et...
It is described to be amongst the greatest environmental crimes and disasters of our time, not to mention the humanitarian disaster it is.
It is a world decimated of life and beauty.
A son of a former engineer (now an emigre engineer living in the US I think) and native of the land, now working with the international community to restore the marsh's through a number of projects (called Eden Again)describes the complexities, which are also testified to by the locals still living there.
'It in a way is not difficult, quite as simple as destroying some embankments and levies and letting the water just flow back into the marshes... in actual reality there are difficulties however... like, will there be enough water to cover the marshes, how will nature be able to regenerate etc...
and from the locals who are not so concerned about the scientific and ecological facts, who now instead want a "fan, a freezer and food"... and can not see what advantages the water will bring to them.
In this example we have everything, re-building, building, regeneration, sequence of necessary and relevant events and actions ie: regeneration, flooding, demolition, relocating people, locating them back etc...(and not necessarily in that order), rediscovering of the pattern languages of the place, environmental catastrophe, human catastrophe, loss of much of the beauty as well as the latent, actual and living centers etc. etc. etc.

Is the solution political, economic, cultural, architectural, ecological, military ?
Obviously its a combination of all, and appart from not really seriously expecting a broad based solution here, what can we say at least for the relevance of the ideas of NOO in this example?
What would be the first thing you would do?

Checking back now I realize how stochastic(shotgun-pattern-gestalt) sharing ideas of this kind is. Gregory Bateson's term, a big mentor of Stewart Brand's. G.B. is way harder to read than me, but thanks for the kind word, and don't worry I'll keep trying.

I leafed through the "How Buildings Learn", once, and remember thinking it was a worthwhile point of view. Maybe if we thought of our art and artifacts enough as organic beings with lives of their own we would encourage them the freedom to morph and grow instead of holding them close and pure like we sometimes do, as extentions of our own inviolate egos.

The thing is Order itself evolves and innovation in the quality of beauty is tied to this rise of complexity and power. The Leanardo, Michealangelo level was not accessible to the cave painter.

At points along the way Order must jump into new phase states, water, ice, cloud is a nice metaphor. Catastrophe is a way of saying one set of rules was strained beyond their limits and a new set took over. House + wind = toothpicks.
Punctuated equilibrium asserts that such things happen periodically, over and over, mainly in the context of wiping the slate almost clean for new starts. Cascade effect: it's dominos and water rushing, tributaries combining, additive stuff reaching critical trigger levels, then the whole thing finally coming to flower, or falling down. Like storm systems, .com bubbles, lemming populations, or political empires.
Later, d.v.

My comment about Nature replacing rather than repairing just refers to how Nature seems to be set up around a lot of birthing. So in that sense, birthing replacement is more the response to damage than fixing. But that is not to say that when it comes to "human" activities I feel we are necessarily bound to duplicate "Nature". Far from it. It seems to me that CA's focus is just on influencing the future births in new architecture in the directions he feels to "have life." Vasco,Brian,
Hitting some good points now we are, thanks for the return to provocative hanging questions, of which you have created a nice handful!

Nature of Order#1&2 is laying out universals, formative abstracts, going way beyond mere architecture, it is an attempt to inform our reductionist mechanistic thought with unifying themes, which to Alexander's credit include Beauty.
This is the context and perspective Alexander has chosen to work through, in my view, using architecture as his wedge tip to access and enrich our larger knowledge of the world.
And this is my reasoning for the discussion always returning here. Because Beauty, and the emotions it is tagged to, is one of only a precious few major languages of nature the cerebral cortex is fluent in.
This "Esthetic Bridge" accesses other truths about the rightness or aliveness of shapes, forms and constructions . Up until now the scientist will only admit elegance, Alexander's work is rare in that he is pushing to develop awareness and acknowledgement of the deeper underlying existence of beauty and ethical content in everything we percieve and create.

Just grazing one of the others:
So right about demolition being "constructive", and the immune system analogy to repair , and evolution akin to remodel. The thought occurs that incompleteness and mistakes should not be deplored, but celebrated as opportunity for furthur evolution in really alive buildings, of course you want value and servicability, but why not welcome eventual changes as inevitable improvement, not original failure.
Maybe it would be good to acknowledge the "temporary" nature of life and buildings and make them easier to change in the first place.
Nature repairing and/or replacing strikes me as a slippery slope, a gradient for sure, ha ,ha, while agreeing with catastrophe theory, cascade effects, punctuated equilibrium etc.
She's very good at what she does, that's for sure.
Top of the morning, Dirk

Greetings Rob, a fellow Bellinghamster!

I put in 20+ years up there '73-'96, and consider it one of the planet's better places for a balanced mix of nature-human, sophisticated-basic. Check out the Taproot pub on the west shore of Lummi Island, or the Beach Store Cafe near the ferry dock, both owned by my old pal Riley Starks who has built several houses and boats and is conversant with Alexander's philosophy of spatial qualities, and the feeling-engendering powers therin.

I haven't posted for awhile, but I see the site here has been lively.

I think Brian's interest in the meaning and character of beauty, and esthetics in general, is at the core of what any discussion in this venue will always return to eventually. The style merchants reference is a great one; let's admit that once functionality and security is satisfied, fashion and style are the great drivers of markets, which in turn direct human endeavor. Architecture is no exception, and the results surround us for better or worse.

The recognition of the profound power of architecture to shape world-view and feeling more than any other human construction is, I think, why Alexander and the rest of us are so interested in it; what it actually is, what it really means, and what makes it good or bad, alive or dead. And of course, the doing of it, as well as we can.

The dialouges have some nice heft, the chatroom is developing into a good forum for some of the bigger ideas, which are curiously absent from most available media.

I look forward to our further explorations..
Salude, Dirk
