brian kennedy 10:16:36 4/08/2004
Rob
Maybe if a virtual reality application could somehow mock up something that FELT "lifesize real" it could be of some use in this process. Maybe it could eventually be done, I imagine that it would take many billions of dollars and many more years before, say, Pixar could develop the necessary details in rendering.
I think that beauty eludes people to the extent that they are averse to the necessary labor involved in bringing it about. Because I believe that is what it takes, simply...first the disciplined labor of learning your chops in the art you devote yourself to, and second the labor of exercising those hard-won chops in the process of co-creation in Reality in the present moment. The creative labor is "painstaking," a sacrifice of time and energy, and also delightful and ecstatic.
What fuels one in such an undertaking is a kind of devotion. What does it take for CA twenty years looking at pairs of pictures 2-3 hours a day? By his confession, he was motivated to perform this sacrifice in order to try to understand how to make something beautiful. And we see his great results so far.
But I think the resort to computers in design to somehow "save time" and "get more done" is a lot of the problem because it breeds superficiality and "rubber-stamping." The disposition that produces rubber-stamping is the antithesis of the one that produces beauty. It's to lazy to do it. It says, "OK, that is good enough, now repeat the module." Whereas, the disposition that produces beauty says, "That is a beautiful center. How can I make a center near it that plays with and off it in such a way that energy is conducted between them synergetically so that they are both enhanced, more alive, more full, more delightful to experience, and they show ther relationship to the whole? That calls for a fresh invention at every stage. More work! But some of us think it is worth it.

brian kennedy From california, usa

http://wwww.thefreedomofcraft.com

Standing there, I couldn't even make out the details at all; they were too far away, yet my brain seemed to recognize the dense clusters of centers. A bit like the carpet experiences mentioned in that section of pl.com

The simple essence is mock it up as big as you can, feel it, shift it around in relationship to other parts until the whole assembly lives and doesn't trip on itself and irritate you, and build it.
CA says people didn't seem to be able to use APL successfully on their own, and he's on sequences as the solution to that. (OK... I'll buy that, in theory.
But basically I think CA is just presenting a brilliant comprehensive argument for the primacy of feeling as the ultimate arbiter in building decisions. That feeling has a universal, "populist dimension" in that anyone can "feel it" for themselves, and it also has an elitist, esoteric dimension--not everyone can pull "Rhapsody In Blue" out of the ether, but when someone does, we can mostly all recognize it and value it as special. CA SORT OF says, follow the sequences I'm going to show you, and you'll get "Rhapsody In Blue"--you can reason out a method to get to inspiration. He also realizes that feeling sensitivity develops with practice, AND that something was going on in that old temple site in Japan where he had that "staged-pre-designed" meeting with the dragonfly that is beyond his present understanding and that is NOT available to be inspected with his thinking mind. Nor can he package and sell that capacity. The most he could do is find out what the practice there was and IF there is a Master available who can transmit it to him If he is willing to meet the requirements of that practice. It is a conceit of the Westerm DIY mentality that Inspiration is reducible to a recipe that the ego can simply grab and appropriate.
Creativity, Originality, Inspiration... these matters are profoundly esoteric.

(quote Mae West I think) I'm only interested in doing unique, very high-end work for clients who also only want and can afford the best, so that is what I do. Yes, every tile is a plate of steak and eggs, and from a very expensive restaurant, to boot. That's good. If you make them pay more, they appreciate it more!

brian kennedy 20:36:59 4/03/2004
Rob
I agree about doing tiles around the windows, budget permitting of course. Making tiles for any application lets you solve the "dutchman problem." I am amazed that people just accept "dutchmen" in tile installations. They are so used to accepting that tiles will not fit evenly in installations without partial cut tiles that even in expensive installations they don't insist on custom tiles being made to blend everything together more smoothly.

It seems to me that windows are a really great opportunity to beautify buildings, sadly, nearly always a missed chance.
Hundertwasser advocated "window rights"-- which he defined as any tenant's right to decorate the exterior wall around his or her window as far as the length of the tenant's arm. He also recommended filling some units in apartment buildings with 3 feet of humus and planting trees that grow out the windows. He said these "tree tenants"(his term) would pay their rent by purifying the atmosphere and absorbing sound. These images first appeared in his paintings and later he carried them out for real. He thought of himself as a doctor for architecture to counteract modernist influences, which he felt were anti-life. (He was also an appreciator of the liveliness of the architecture in "slums.")
Brian Kennedy

brian kennedy From lake county ca, usa

Rob Asumendi 17:05:57 4/03/2004
I imagine that the relative success of roughness depends on its intent. The way I understand it, roughness comes about a) as natural consequence of paying attention to the unique configuration of existing centers and b) intentionally to soften the "brutal" rigidity of designed form, e.g. skyscraper windows. From Mr. Alexander's discussions of the tile layout in NOO 1 & 2, it seems the wall itself was the center being strengthened by the tiles, not the windows. He mentions gradients as the driving transformation here, not roughness. It seems the windows are strengthened by the empty space around them, perhaps void comming into play. So the roughness of the tiling pattern here is a consequence of keeping these voids equal size for uniquely positioned windows. I think the photo on page 295 shows this decision in better light than the photo on 287, because at this scale you can see that the gray area creates a better boundary for the window. I guess one would have to see the wall without the tiles before judging whether the tiles actually damage the windows as centers. I can see how choosing to lay the tiles to the very edges of the windows would have resulted in a "wallpaper" look, weakening the windows AND the walls as centers. Perhaps an even better solution would have been some more elaborate tile work surrounding the windows to give them a thicker membrane/boundary, but who knows, that could have blown the budget.

As for the plum tree, funny how a little plum tree can become so famous :)

Hi, btw. I live in NW Washington, not too far from the Medlock house... maybe I can catch a glimpse sometime, make sure they're keeping up the yardwork.

Vasco
When I asked, "when is roughness too rough," I was thinking of the situation around the windows on the Julian Street building NOO 2 page 287. I think that CA might have thought it was OK to handle the pattern of squares around those windows that way by invoking the "roughness" principle. But this resolution is to me very unsatisfactory, (even though it was judged to be the better of two ideas during the working out of the design), because it is a poor "boundary" for the windows, one that cuts arbitrarily into "positive space" around the window. Or maybe another way to say it is that the windows are unneccessarily weak centers because they need a stronger border around them--as in the example of the borders around the doors of a Gothic Cathedral elsewhere in NOO, (can't find the page right now.)
I think that CA's designs tend to have decorative touches that are too rote, too "rubber-stamped" in their quality. That is to say, they need to have more variations in the parts as they go along, say, a wall. I know he is sensitive to this need and maybe it was just a budget limit on the project, but just as he advocates the charm of "aperiodic grid" in plan layout, a "periodic grid" or rubberstamp repitition of a geometric pattern or even the lily pattern on the Upham house is not life-producing, to my sense of it. The "every part unique" principle is being abandoned, at a big loss of life in such a case.

Ih Tiao Chang 10:34:25 4/01/2004
Beijing, one of the famous historic and cultural cities of China, is also one of the lasting architectural miracles in the history of the world's civilization.

Especially those narrow Hutongs(lanes) arouse many reveries, and it takes a lot of learning to explore into their developments.

Hutongs of Beijing are decidedly not only the historical and present viens and capillaries of the city, but also the site where families live, and the important arena of a perfection which was reached in single story, high density, low cost residential architecture.

The successful happiness of the owners and renters and their love of the individual houses and neighborhoods are the essential evidence that the "pattern complex" is a good one.

The highly sustainable character over the centuries and present day desireability ensures that they are even more valuable to us now as we attempt to find ways, within our own time and culture, to make houses and lanes that are alive again, and capable of satisfying people on deep emotional levels.

The courtyard houses of the old hutongs are more in tune with nature, thus illustrating the concept of people as a part of nature and proposing an ideal alternative in the center of a large bustling metropolis, able to apply in many diverse global situations.

Many, very many, aspects of the lives of people and buildings are universally true everywhere, in all ages.

"It is a kind of enjoyment to sit under the grape vine in sweltering summer."

"Roofs designed like surging waves, white with snow."

A saying goes among old Beijing residents that, in Beijing, 360 Hutongs are given a name each, while those without names are beyond calculation.

Ih Tiao Chang From Hyatt Grand Hotel, Beijing, China

Hmmm! don't know Chris.
Unfortunately it's not like knitting a jumper, once you finish it it's done... for better or worse, whether it has enough borders and voids or not.
With a jumper, you can easily tell... if theres a gaping hole somewhere, you see it and do something to repair it.
My own opinion at this stage is not to worry about it too much... at most, I stand back, squint my eyes and look at it through half closed,or half open eyes and say, yeah that looks like a 'field of centers', or a nice pattern atleast. At this stage this is the extent of my 'scientific' approach, squint at it!
At this stage, I'm still grappling with applying the patterns, and coming to terms with 'sequences'. So, I suppose in this respect, I'm a traditionalist and believe that if you apply the patterns, and sequences correctly, then its also likely there will be enough of the 15 principles present. Gauging the success of an applied set of patterns is easier for me than counting centers (I'm reading that sometimes there can be up to 40,000 of them).
This brings me again to my other question that I am interested in... about sequences and the application of patterns to actually existing structures, to an actually existing world largely going through what is commonly agreed to be a degenerative process in this period of human history.
If NOO talks about 'generative sequences', and PL's as being similar to that of genetic information, why do they not talk about the process of repair being akin to the immune system also... insofar as it takes the role of repairing degenerative processes?
Getting back to practical matters.
Most of my work, about 60-70% is working with existing buildings, and here I reckon that the process is subtly different to that of building from new. I can not describe how it is precisely different, but I would like to try.
1. Often you don't start from the largest patterns, but might start with the smaller ones
2.The process of demolition occupies the sequences very early on. This phenomenon to me feels different to the generative process of 'creating life', and I would say is closely related to the repairing process and the 'structure preserving' transformations. The activity of demolition however is rarely mentioned in CA's ideas, appart from his descriptions of it in natural processes, ie: the breaking of waves, collapsing of cylinders etc...
Indeed in some projects, the demolition process is sufficient in extent and nature to suffice for a greatly repaired situation. Anything more in these circumstances is imposing order. In this respect, I'm viewing the act of demolition or destruction as an act of 'building' also, or at least as generative/creative as the process of creation/ construction.

Anyway, getting back to your question. Why do you want to know this question? What is the concrete or actual excercise that you are applying?

brian kennedy 13:28:20 3/27/2004
Vasco,
Regarding your first point, I would say that your letter has more life than many so far and the practical value of this is that you have "enlivened" the proceedings here. To me, the constant tacit fact about human beings is that they are always interested in more life and always chose that direction if they can discern which way it lies. Thus there is nothing MORE PRACTICAL than this matter.
Your experience with the question seems the same as CA's--he seems to have run into alot of opposition at Berkeley, for instance, when he used the question to "empirically" show his students, "measured" by inspecting their own HEART-FEELING (and what a taboo there is against that!) that modernism had become an emperor who had no clothes.
Incidently, another great innovator that got bounced out of there (UC) was Henry Schaeffer-Simmern, author of "The Unfolding of Artistic Activity" because he had the temerity to suggest that Abstract Expressionist painting (which at that time was sweeping the art world such that ,one by one, all the other members of the faculty dropped what they were doing and became "Abstract Expressionists") was not art, because art was not "self-expression." Rather, it might more aptly be called "Self-expression." And I notice that in his attempt to explain his method of finding his way to and through sequences of decisions CA is resorting to Buddhist, including specifically Zen Buddhist, language. Zen is a tradition of Transmission outside of what we call mind. So, while we can have interesting discussions about this empiricism of the feeling sense, it probably will not reduce itself to a form that can be completely corralled, roped, and tied by word-based Cartesian logic. It is very taboo to suggest this, so in vol. 1 of NoO CA makes a big point of showing very clearly that he is a great respector and practioner of the mathematical-scientific approach to the investigation of Reality. BUT, he very respectfully shows why he has to step beyond it to account for and cure the psychosis he sees in architecture of the last fifty years.
As to the practical value of CA's work to anyone designing and making anything, it seems to me obvious. Consider a simple song "I want To Hold Your Hand" by The Beatles. When this song appeared it was utterly simple, utterly original, and utterly electrifying. It swept the market and made the musicians celebrities almost overnight. If you do creative work, you are interested in tapping What that song tapped. And if you do, you will win the Pritzker? Award very soon. And you will succeed in releasing profoundly beneficial enlivening energy into the world. I think what CA is doing is presenting information that will enable that, big-time.

Chris,

I have been reading the NOO book two,and yes there are quite a number of issues about practicality that I would be interested in... after all, as you point out,whats the purpose of the theory?
I'll also be willing to play the devils advocate, or the village idiot, and ask or raise some issues which might be obvious to many, so please excuse my ignorance and the dumb questions.
I was just thinking today, that I'm still not convinced about the NOO books, at the danger of making an ignorant and hasty judgement (at the same time I do think they are remarkable and I have been bowled over by them in many ways so far. I'm also still wondering whether the PL are better in some ways).
1. About 'life'. Someone a little while ago raised the point on this site about the question Alexander asks about 'what has most life?'. I was struck by this question, of Alexanders... its simplicity, its directness, its obviousness, its power.
I am therefore interested in this question itself (amongst many others). What potential does this question have in practicality?
At one stage, i nearly divided the office i used to work in. I asked the question, in a very similar way Alexander asks it in his book, over a series of photographs i had taken around the office and the neighbourhood.
At another stage, i turned clients around with by asking the question, who were nearly going to make what i thought was going to be a stupid decision about their house plan. It managed with a single stroke, so to speak, to break a long impasse, particularly over some issues about aesthetics and style we had been discussing for a while.
2.About sequences. I still haven't seen any sequences for actually existing buildings. How do they work here?
Most of the examples are about from scratch building and design. Is there a chasm in the theory between 're-building the earth' and the actual content, focus and actual applicability of sequences.
In relation to this issue, i still fail to see the major step forward from PL, which at the outset make it clear that patterns, in a pattern language are themselves ordered (ie: from largest to smallest etc...)
3. About patterns. Part of this question has a more philosophical aspect, and that is the empirical nature of patterns, and infact, much of the theory of PL and NOO, seems to have empiricism as one of its backbones... or at least this seems to be one of the legitimising factors for patterns.
What about patterns that don't have empirical validation or evidence?
4. Modularisation. I recently worked with a firm (using PL methodology) who I thought definitely had an aspect of modularisation. This seems strange to me. Another related example is 'off the shelf' plans, which PL themselves offer for sale. How are these reconciled with PL methodology, particularly in terms of generating site specific, culturally responsive buildings and environments, or with the 'step by step' approach.
5. The 'unfolding process' of the internet?
How has this website influenced the natural, 'unfolding process' of a lively discussion and debate. (sorry to bark on old ground with this one).
I might be totally wrong, but it strikes me, that may the dynamics, or the dialectic between this site and its users be rendering a discussion, perceived and described by yourself as just idle in terms of making concrete and practical steps towards changing, or 're-building' the world. (this is by no means an attempt at isolation and levelling of critism at users... more a point about the form influencing the content. This is not so strange since NOO, unlike the TWOB or PL, starts with geometry, whereas the earlier books started with patterns).
6. Education. I liked your earlier idea about running PL workshops. It also strikes me that there is no resource or directed attempt to teach people how to write, or collect patterns. To me, being able to apply and invent relevant and current pattern languages for people and communities, architects, designers, builders, anyone interested in using Pl as a process should know how to write patterns.

Feel free to add your own issues about what sort of practical issues you are thinking of.

Hey Chris
Thanks for the lead on the article, I enjoyed it, although I enjoy CA's critiques alot more for their radical "beyond Cartesian" depth and range of examples from art. I find CA's analysis to be extremely wide-ranging and at the same time so profound that it moves me more to contemplation than to discussion. I think his strengths as a "noticer" and "understander" are wonderfully developed, more so than his abilities as a "generator" when it comes to beauty. I am so glad he is on the side of the good guys! His notion of transforming the totalitarian democratric grip on building processes by introducing snippable genes for better ideas that can slowly gain piecemeal acceptance is the first hope I have had in many years that the ugliness and feelinglessness ruling architecture can be overcome.
What interests me personally right now is understanding the nature of the "roughness" property. When is rough too rough and when is the design not rough enough? I find Islamic designs usually unsatisfactory because their geometric quality is too precise. (Although their use of calligraphy in architecture is a good counterfoil.)I love it when a geometric design "swims" a little bit--as in what CA calls an "aperiodic grid." There is an amazing book called "Who'd a Thought It?" about Afro-American quilts by Eli Leon. He only collects quilts that have improvisational play in their patterning. Such quilts have "life" to a high degree as CA discusses so well. In Architecture, the work of Josep Maria Jujol, who was Gaudi's assistant but an artist-engineer-architect in his own right, shows me tremendous life. I am studying CA and Jujol together to help me understand Beauty better. It's late, gotta go, hope this advances the effort of getting a little interchange going, or at least mutual offering of leads we may find illuminating...

His tool kit is always tidy and clean in them two well-worn carry bags, and he's somehow got the right fitting 'n pipe dope right there, when we need it.
But the one I really like is how he hand washes his coupla pair of wool socks every night or two and hangs 'em up to dry under that little back porch overhang.
So far as the plumbin' goes, near as I can make out for him it's some kinda reverance or pride for what the wall of the pipe can do. Sometimes if you get him goin just right he'll talk about the miracle of seperatin' two worlds, or one world servin the needs of another, or sumpthin' suchlike.
I don't quite see it yet myself, like he does, but he don't dwell on it long, mainly he's happy bein' good at plumbin'.


Utopian Plumber

Dirkk Visser 00:10:17 3/23/2004
"I think I would say it a little differently than that."
The Gradient.
Why is there always polarity?
Oh, everything in Nature is a gradient. Gradient is one of those forms we need to consider-like symetry.
In fact one of the forms of symetry would be a gradient.
Nature uses the gradient everyplace, its how simple things become complex, how indeterminate things become rigorous, or how young things become old, see, the gradient theme is throughout Nature.
And the gradient is not an escaladient. It can be utilized in an escaladient but it is not the concept of an escaladient.

You can have a gradient fron the simple to the complex, from the less to the more intelligent if you will.
The gradient has two seemingly finite ends, taken in time it shows what exists, but it is essentially a report.

The escaladient on the other hand is dynamic, it must have innovation within it. Innovation in a gradient would be fatal. It would destroy it.
The escaladient aims at being, the gradient is only part of the process. But it is one of the key structures of nature-The Gradient.
Alexander sees it, from his vantage point, and describes it well. It's not a tough one to get agreement on, and works as a good non-threatening lead to more meaningful underlying structures.

Mahalo Amigos, D.V.

Dirkk Visser From Niles,Ca., USA

(wrote The Unfolding of Artistic Activity), Adi Da Samraj, Hundertwasser.
I have one technical question/observation: there is a man in Arizona who has come up with a technique he calls "cast earth" in which he adds plaster to soil and retards the hardening process long enough to be able to pour an entire building shell at once. I like the looks of the finished wall alot but he is not making his process available to the small-scale user, he is going in the other direction. I would love to see if I could use a similar process small-scale, letting the stuff harden as I went in small batches, (sort of like the rammed earth/pise technique where they have a pretty small form that they move frequently instead of a large one that they do the whole wall at one time.) That's on my wish list If anyone has some ideas about it I'd love to hear.
Brian Kennedy

Ih Tiao Chang 23:54:31 3/16/2004
Monumental Chinese architecture was traditionally laid out according to cosmological principles that became known as feng shui- away of arranging objects to avoid or deflect the invasion of evil forces.
the Daoist Buddhist and Confucian temples you see in China today are a product of these principles.
Protected by rectangular wallslike a small city the temples main entrance faces south.
Inside the massive wooden doors is a series of pavillions and couryards, lined on both sides by residential and administrative halls. The inner complex can be compact or vast, consisting of hundreds of buildings or a small handful.
The temple architecture is virtually identical regardless of the religion practiced within. Variations in style are a product of region and historical period, not the dictates of faith.
The main support consists of interlocking wooden beams and enormous columns resting on raised platforms. The curving roof is paved in ceramic tiles. In southern China elaborate ornamentation and increasingly upturned eaves became the fashion. In the north temple architecture remained more restrained.

Ih Tiao Chang

Vasko Drogriski 17:09:24 3/03/2004
Chris
Yes, only a little in Australia. Lots of information around about it but not much experience using it.
My limited experience of it is that its labour intensive, particularly the rendering part. The bales can go up quickly but you have to watch out for lateral stability, as well as detailing around doors and windows. There is also the issue of load bearing or infill... load bearing is difficult, bales need to have great consistency in compactness, moisture content, particular straw should be used etc...
The general consensus down here seems to be it is particularly good for:
Rural situations where the bales are availabe and dont have to be transported long distances
Good for extremely cold and extremely hot climates
The bales have infinte insulation and no thermal mass, therefore around here they are often used as external walls
Not good for:
Damp climates
Urban buildings as the bales take up too much space.
I think they require more specialisation and experience than is thought.
Since the introduction of straw bale down here, there have been other 'straw' based waste products also, such as straw board panels. These are used as an internal cladding, much easier and faster to use.
Very early on I talked about my experience of how bad 'patterns' are so persistent in our culture.
I have had some more recent experiences and was wondering what others do about them.
The particular pattern I am talking about is 'Backyards'.
Just recently I completed a house design with clients, fairly young, in which we used many of CA's patterns... starting off with the major ones of siting, locating gardens in the best, sunniest positions, working through circulation realms and main room positions etc... The product was a great design. We also used some patterns that we invented ourselves, such as 'south facing outdoors', which is shade.
One day, the clients came to me after talking to friends and family, showing off their design we had made, and were told by people that they had no 'backyard'. Ofcoarse, they did have a backyard, but the point is it was not in the usual place, opposite in direction to the front yard, or directly behind the home. It was instead along the side of the home, on the sunny side.
This realisation was so powerfull, that all of a sudden the clients forgot much of the previous process and now were fixated on the problem of 'not having their Backyard'. The pattern of the backyard, like many other patterns, is so consistently repeated and deeply ingrained and entrehched as a pattern, that it pulls extremely hard, away from what I understand this 'unfolding process' to be, or away from people to do naturally what they should. I realise that this is no revelation to anyone here. But I am interested to know if there are any others who have had similar experiences with 'bad patterns' and what these patterns are.
I don't mean to divert attention from the current trajectory of the discussions, but I find that these more mundane everyday problems do not leave me free enough to think about the next level of discussion.

Vasko Drogriski From Melbourne, Australia

Dirk Visser 01:08:05 2/29/2004
Thanks for the related sites Chris, it's good to know there is some kindred discussion around.

Your question kicks it good. I'll take a quick shot at it hoping that we'll get some follow up. I'm sure as poser of the query you will elaborate on it,(smile).

"Structure preserving process", may be another way to say, "order which endures and evolves". If we start looking at the built and natural environment this way, as Alexander has, the mystery of what underlies and holds it together is the natural next waypoint. Visualize Logic as an agent actively scrutinizing Order for inefficiencies as it evolves, and slowly weeding them out. "Order by trial and error is just as good as Order by dictum." It takes time out of mind, but there seems to have been enough.

I submit to you that an event is not just the measurable wieghing and measuring of a change as we all agree. I assert that along with this, an event(or object) is also a tightly knotted analgam of esthetic and ethical readouts as well as the easily seen physical mechanistic evidence.

This is a key point, largely unseen, even by Alexander, but when one finally grasps that nature is set up this way, everything becomes threefold deeper and we begin to be able to read the Order for ethically helpful content, not just physically empowering facts, or disconnected beauty.

Let me digress slightly, sorry. The point is that any latent center with the power to raise conciousness is not some esoteric secret, it is only a lead to the realization that we are nature's best accomplishment on the road to extending and elaborating the capability of feeling, imagination, and critical interpretation. Evolution flows into all the niches and nature doesn't play favorites ,she's after what we've got at the moment , but don't kid yourself, the dry chip is coming right along now (with our help), and you can bet the amount of nurture and energy expenditure is compareable to what was lavished on every other part of the word which succeeds, including ourselves.
It helps to anthropomorphize sometimes: "Nature is insanely proud of, and jealously guards the intelligence of a gnat."

Ironically' I think the "latent center" you speak of is not a center at all in the Alexanderan sense. The next most powerful dicovery which seems to be in progress now as we discuss these ideas is the realization of the connectedness of all things, and dawning awareness of the boundary controls that become visible (and debilitating) as the systems are pushed to maximum saturation or maximum extraction. This bumping up against the edge is getting our attention now and in the next couple of centuries will force our attention back toward your latent centers which have been lost, forgotten, or never before found.

Myth will not do it this time. The noble values of our Buddha, Christ, Mohammed, Great Spirit, and all the rest will not be lost, they will be more powerful and secure than ever because these values will flow directly from the content of nature itself, once we learn to read her intent and meaning.

And learn it we will, or it's curtains for humanity.

Your question remains, I realize, and a damn good one it is.

Christopher Loyd 12:50:59 2/01/2004
I've tried for the first time in years to apply CA's ideas to design, in an architectural setting. I won't go into details (e-mail me if you have any questions), but here's what I've learned:

1) CA is known, though seen as kooky in contemporary education. I use "kooky" as a catch-all term, because everyone has differing opinions of the man, though it's easy enough to notice that openingly talking about CA is like talking about Bible. People either go really quiet, or a fight erupts.

2) When using APL, use the methodoly according to the book. Focus on one pattern at a time, in a given language sequence. I selected no more than ten patterns, and have applied three.

3) Don't mention patterns by name. Do talk about the desired trait or result. You want arcades because you want a transitional space between the outside and the inside, or whatever. Sell your idea in whatever way you can to get the design that is the best.

4) Keep it simple. Don't go onto the next pattern until you have fulfilled the first one. Build small, simple models, adding detail as time goes on.

5) Go with the flow. If the instructor suggests something different, go ahead and incorporate it, unless it completely contradicts what you're trying to do. For me, I've gone along with his suggesting, APL-izing it the whole time.

6) Don't make enemies. If anyone asks you why you're doing what you're doing, explain simply that people like XYZ, and that you want to accomplish that, hence your design. If you format what you're saying into something objective ("People like views"), it forces the other person to consider your statement as FACT, not OPINION. If they disagree, inquire into the facts. By keeping the discussion factual, you can only argue by what is known, not believed. Conversations usually don't go far. The other person loses interest. Who wants to talk about the study that showed that x-amount of people preferred 'Y'?

I haven't presented my project to a public audience. That will happen tomorrow. I will post the results of that.

Christopher Loyd From Houston, USA

http://www.geocities.com/tybalt1982

Dirk visser 00:02:21 1/29/2004
Well said, but your question begs for the completion link, the perfect alpha-omega circularity, eh?...:no clown, no mind!.., right?...heh,heh

A welcome relief to manage a little chuckle after a day or so of working up in the drafting loft trying to formulate a topography of Logic which could fit down over nature with sufficient accuracy to approach the axiomatic.

It's not about "the two dyad calculus, truth derivatives, or even the logic of statement."

It starts more like: If you see Natural Law operating, there sure as hell has to be something holding it together. That something is connectivity, immutable unchanging, rigor, that which allows relations: Logic.
The topography is only there as a reflection of the response matter and energy have generated over deep time to the initial intent and the endless subtle "leaning on" that has given direction.

Strong as Nature is she cannot do this in the blink of an eye. We read intent now by the results, The flat featureless empty logic of the creation has become the rich subtle complex matrix that nature and culture show us today. This matrix is an accessible readout of purpose and progress through the 4 domains of Order. We read Order for everthing we know.

The readout will show an unmistakeable rise of complexity, and sensitivity and interaction through time.
This truth has profound implications for the human place in the continuum.

The periodic table of elements was not an easy thing to develop, even given all the hydrogen and time one needs. That was the begining of another wonderful intangible thing we now possess:..........Patience.....

See you clowns next Wednesday, Dirk

Dirk Visser 04:08:06 1/11/2004
Hmmm...Maybe...clown?..heh,heh...Well, looking at it that way, are you suggesting that death is some kind of invention to permit the possibility of life itself..?

Anyway, back to how great architecture is done:

In February 1921, Morgan wrote to Hearst about an idea for constructing some steps at San Simeon so the guests would have an memorable view of the main building:
While standing at the point (A) yesterday and looking up imagining the towers, the fine "looming up" effect grew on me-and the regret that few would see it often from this point. Then it occured to me, why not... continue the steps on down...a strikingly noble and "saississant" effect would be impressed upon everyone on arrival.

Hearst noted in the margin:

Heartily approve those steps. I certainly want that saississant effect. I don't know what it is but I think we ought to have at least one such on the premises.

(Saississant is a French word meaning "gripping".)

That architect-client relationship endured for another 25 years, a continuation of the highly succesful work Morgan had already done for Hearst's mother Phoebe, in the preceding two decades.

I see it's check out time again..Bienvenidos, Dirk

The opposable thumb with a grip of twenty tons.

Nature has trained and shaped us for a game we no longer need to play, in fact a game which will kill us unless we can learn to let it go. She has not taught us for the new game, we must figure it out ourselves, because we now finally, after geologic time, at a taking control level...a thought level.

In a Universe like this one, the critical danger point happens here, where the newly thinking species gains tremendous power through primitive reason over the nature which has incubated it.

Philosophy is the broadest possible application of reason. It's traditional role is to inform power, which the advances of science, now bled off directly to technology, represent.


Similar to the way Alexander is attempting to tell the secrets of architecture and incude the mental, affective side in the essential roots and results, other sciences will also begin to look at the underlying structure of Order and the implications for decisions determining the structure of our world. I count myself fortunate to have grown up with her buildings as ideals......

Keep drawing, keep building, and for sure, keep thinking....Salutation, Dirk

Chris, Appreciation for the cogent response, New Year affirmation to you.
In 1962, as a kid I visited the Hearst Castle in San Simeon, on the Central coast of California. Last week, with a short lifetime of experience in-between I made a return trip. The accomplishment of Hearst, his architect Julia Morgan, and the 25 years of efforts by the building crew is as full of value and inspiration as anything you are ever likely to witness. As a 14 year old I was impressed certainly, as a 55 year old it was more like a pride that such a thing could still be done by us as late as mid-Twentieth Century...

About Das Linz Cafe, I had similar feelings to yours when I first got into it, and sometimes I still find myself unmoved, but you will warm slowly over time and as the realization of how quickly and cheaply the thing was accomplished. In some ways it is a wonderful little demonstration building precisely because of it's shortcomings and lack of integration! There is a very clear illustration of the "Aperiodic Grid" based on the linz cafe in the Book Two on Order, which I have been enjoying immensely since Christmas. You really owe it to yourself, regardless of the expense.
These works mark a maturity of thought missing from the earlier work...and you can't miss it.

Sorry to cut short, I'll try to get back on here later tonight with some furthur thoughts on the Julia Morgan legacy.

Thinking , Feeling, Action.

The Three Modes of experiential learning.
When we begin to delve into The Event and how the mind interprets it, reads Order into it, and gets meaning out of it, this is a key aspect of the adaptive process operating.
ALL EVENTS ARE STRUCTURED. And they are structured in a reflection of the original descriminatory criteria which limited the possibilities from an infinity of choices.
This Universe, this Unity is specific and limited, and therein lies its character and meaning. Therin lies the basis of its 4 interpretive Domains of Order:

# 1. The Rational,[(all of science), we can go on wieghing and measuring things forever, without knowing what they are for]. Science has this fatal flaw: it is value-free, it refuses to make conclusions, powerful for excluding error, but meaningless standing alone.... **(Strongly tied to thinking)**

#2. The Esthetic ( all of art ) Beauty is a powerful informer and bridge between atifact and mind. it tells clearly when things approach perfection in nature and in human craft, but it too falls short of fulfilling meaning taken by itself.... **(Strongly tied to feeling)**

#3 The Ethical (the relationship, supportive behavior to hold the Unity in place) our least developed side as emerging primitive thinkers with the power of free will. **(Strongly tied to Action)**
***************************
There's more , there's always more, but my kid is hounding me to play tennis, a reflection of the crowded nature of space and time the inevitable conflicts which occur in a crowded system. The boulder falls from the cliff and crushes the tree. Does this mean nature is illogical or unethical? ......... We continue to misread this one; it only means that in a complex crowded space and system there will be competition and conflict. It is a hard Order, struggle is part of the character here.......... Once seen clearly this is a cause for celebration, Taken further it implies a job description for an intelligent species.

Chris Derrington 19:27:54 12/01/2003
Just to throw out some more views, something to chew on, or to discuss further, in relation to the contents of this website...

The architectural Golden Rule?:
Always design it as if it were going to be built right next door to your home.

This thought is still in development- but you'd be surprised how often the exact opposite of this thought is enacted in our communities.

Every act of creation must begin with goodwill. Without this element, the action will ultimately be destructive.
This applies in all cases.
*Goodwill being a benevolent concern or interest that extends beyond oneself.

Chris Derrington From San Antonio, US of A

D. Visser 02:25:15 12/01/2003
If, for the sake of convenience, we do what all known philosophers of the past 5,000 years have done, and divide Order into the four domains of Rationality, Esthetics , Ethics , and Spirituality we can account for all aspects of the observable.
There's only one small problem: The imperatives of order: repetition, rhythmn, symetry, proportion,equalibrium, harmony, Alexander's 15 "properties", and most likely a few undiscovered ones, only operate on the fist three domains.
When you cross over to the spirituality side the ball game has rules like; love, hope, faith, and charity.
If we,re really looking at Order, we've got to look at it all, trouble is, like the old man in Maine said, giving directions, "You can't get there from here"
Here,s the problem: The order that we are so busy interpreting has all nicely evolved since the big bang, guided and shaped by the aforementioned properties and imperatives. That takes care of physical, artistic, and even behavioral, order pretty well. But it cannot even get a start explaining why human beings know the world is unfinished, know that something irrational like love or faith is worth losing everything, or dying for. And dont't forget humor....
Beautiful is the rainbow, but on earth it takes the human mind to say so and assign a value like "hope". It's not "in there" without us fellas.
Like better minds before me I've turned it over a few times, the monkey does love to look at the crystal. After a while though you start to see; this is the stuff that was here first. These feelings and ideas, that we are only now finally in the last few hundred generations beginning to gain access to, must be antecedent to the creation.
That right there is the kicker: only recently showing up here- around before big bang-maybe there is a little something to this animal that thinks.
This is "the twist" for humanity and for science to come to terms with. It's a windfall for religion, but a serious rewrite of the script, informed by what we have figured out over the past 300 years, is the only way to cash the chips in.
We can't go on much longer this way compadres. We've got to use what we got, that's Knowledge and Belief, both deadly forces. It's getting late quick, but we can still get these two together and they can have a helluva life together, and so can the planet after all..........

I haven't paid for membership for this site, and I'm a practically a diciple of Alexander's work. Think of how unlikelly it is that the mildly interested will do so.

Success at bringing a group together around some concrete project or activity seems to often hinge on the participants having, like Craig said a week or two ago, some kind of consequential stake in the project. Teamwork and the resulting comraderie of mutual dependance are powerful drivers during the process while a shared goal or vision of an improved future provides long term fuel for the effort.
Ownership starts at the conceptual phase as well as the first groundbreaking. One of the tricks is to walk the edge between inclusivity for recruitment, and exclusivity to feed the in-crowd pride strokes.

Crooked Little Man 23:22:41 11/24/2003
I enjoy the abstract ruminations, but I'm too busy most of the time. Here are a few more hot tips from my kit box for you hands on architect types.
Crown moulding is a beautiful thing, the upside down and backwards part adds mystique, as does the $500 compound mitre saw, but there are a couple of other ways:
1) The hand coping saw along a line scribed by holding a dummy piece cut at the magic 31.6 degree angle.
2) The protracter saw guide adjustable square set at 31.6 to give your hand held power saw set on its own 31.6 bevel the straight and true compound angle you're after.

Big,generous base boards are another thing out of fashion now that go a long way toward a comfortable room.
Don't overlook the possibility of the oversize half-round spline under the trim lintel as a cheap way to add a subtle touch of distinction to door and window casements, alternatively the plain or embossed corner square also kicks some high-end bandicoot, for next to nothing.
A quick thought that may help on tile floor layouts: Create your pattern like a carpet leaving a nice plain "field tile" border. You can do all your fudging and uneveness here and it won't show especially if you use larger stuff. What can really make this trick work and boost your design's power is to lay in an intermediate border between your design and the field, takes the heat off both sides and really looks like you're dialed into the ancient masters! Speaking of baseboards, a tile one is the only sensible one in a wet place like the bathroom, While you're at it, reward yourself, plug up your tub overflow outlet and see what a full bath can really be, freedom can happen in small ways too, remember?
Good Day!

Crooked Little Man From his crooked little house, N.S.W.

A crooked little man 22:35:15 11/18/2003
I like to build it my way!
An eight penny nail works as well as a twist drill for predrilling, then use the phillips bit for a quick countersink.
A four inch disc sander is just about like a router for rounding over or chamfering boards.
Trim boards with generous reveals are more forgiving and add strong interest.
Mitered corners always open up at the heel used outdoors. A plain 90 degree joint will stay tight for years.
Saw for clear wood and use short lengths butted to longitudinals on 2',3',or 4' centers for siding, wainscoating or ceilings, ask the Japanese if you don't believe me.
An outdoor bathhouse is best, attatched or free standing, otherwise put an exterior door in the bathroom accessing a private deck or small porch.
The kitchen is the active heart of the home, give it visual access/authority over important parts of interior living and important outdoor space.
A dutch-style split back or side door is a very good thing, if you are brave enough to try it, such a door in the right circumstance can transform the interior and exterior spaces it serves and can bestow great peace and satisfaction with one's house.
A skylight usually works better than anticipated
Houses that are comepletely encicled by a nice walkway are quite rare, but generally very pleasant to spend time in and around.
The most valuable thing about many great houses, expensive and cheap, is how they relate to their surroundings, from within and without.
Linseed or Tung oil cut with turpentine is an excellent treatment for exterior wood furniture, fences, decks, arbors, and siding, and it can be pigmented for some great effects you will never find at the store.
When you stretch a design element past the conventional limit it will generally continue to work--but, it will require supporting suroundings to make changes to fit the new effect, to re-establish a new harmony.
Wood is nice ,White is nice, and so is color. Mix in some stone, tile, and brick. Pay a lot of attention to the light and sight lines and a good house begins to appear. Maybe built perfectly level and straight, maybe not, The more you design and build, the better you and your designs will get, that's just how it works!

A crooked little man From his crooked little house, N.S.W

Craig Ambrose 15:33:06 11/13/2003
Your comments make me smile Dirk.

I'm going to stop talking about alternatives to this website for now, there'll be time enough for that later.

Let's talk about pattern language itself perhaps. For me, the idea of pattern language itself is important, but not life changing. It's a way of representing problems which I guess I was already familiar with (being a computer programmer), and I know that to some extend this was fueled by Alexander's work, but I'm not entirelly convinced that other people haven't had similar ideas independantly. Certainly the entire "think globally. act locally" philosophy carries much the same idea.

Likewise, the other ideas contained in "The Timeless Way of Building" are very interesting, and I generally agree with them, but also not life changing. If it were just for this book, I would discard Alexander's work as being merelly a usefull set of ideas for designing buildings, but certainly not something which I should be advocating in my spare time.

But then there's the pattern language which Alexander et al present in "A Pattern Language", and I think that it's worth saying that these ideas are tremendously emportant, life changing event. It's generally this book that I push on people, even though it's the second in the series, because when people recognise the universal truth (at least within our culture) of some of these patterns, then much of the ancillary ideas surrounding pattern language in general become self-evident.

Besides, if you want to inspire people to change, you need to show them practical ideas which can be implimented, and ideally have been implimented, not waffle about abstract ideas and fractals. :)

So I was thinking to myself a couple of days ago, I haven't done much about this recently. I figure what I might do is offer a free short course at the local community house, presenting a bunch of patterns from the book, and with some group exercises to analyse some of the public areas around the community house with respect to what patterns they apply, and which extra ones could be squeezed in order to give them even more life.

Maybe out of that I'll find enough interested local people that we can form a bit of a local pattern language club, and actually go round applying some design patterns in public areas around the town, or at each others houses.

So although I enjoy the abstract discussion, I've had enough of it, and I want to get my hands dirty.

And here on the internet, there's a place for that too. I'd like to see more research done, not by boffins, but by regular people, on improving the language.

We need to be using collaberative tools for pattern language development. Computer programmers have been doing this for years now with their pattern languages. Things like wiki's are perfect for this. He's an example:

http://c2.com/ppr/

That one is, as I said, programming patterns, which aint going to change the world any time soon, but the methods they use to develop them will work for us too.

That's todays rant. :) Your work sounds interesting. My day's mainly going to consist of sitting in front of the computer, although I do have a couple of hours of woodwork to do this morning.

take care,

Craig

Craig Ambrose From Melbourne, Australia

Craig Ambrose 22:21:12 11/12/2003
Hi there Dirk,

You asked how I got nice paragraphs? Well the short answer is that I pressed enter twice every time I finished a paragraph. Are you unable to do that on your machine? On to more serious matters, yes it is a triffle quiet here.You talk about working together to figure out how to work it. As far as I can see though, it's not you or anyone else who needs to learn how to use this site. You're doing great. It's the site itself that's broken.

I didn't want to sound too negative in my last post, and I certainly don't want to sound like I'm personally attacking the developers of this site. However, this site is plainly so confusing, difficult to use, and generally obfuscated that I believe that is more of a hinderance to the pattern language movement then a help, and has been for many years. Certainly with no more than a day or two of my time, I could put together something infinitelly better. I'm not bragging here, so could most other web developers.

In fact, even a simple discussion group at yahoo would be infinitelly more usefully to me than this site. However, perhaps I have different motivations to the people running this site. I want to change the world for the better. Sure, I want to see Alexander and his colleages renumerated for their clever work. I've bought their books at significant expense (and they are worth every sent), and I encourage others to do the same. However, I see this as a fairly mundane secondary consideration compared to this 'changing the world' business.

So this information, these ideas, need to be disseminated more widelly. Used, discussed, put into practice in my backyard, and my neighbours back yard, to transform my home town, and my home city, and any other city that wants to join in.

When I visit this site, I see a few words to this effect, but the overall feeling I get is one of holding back, of shutting people out. Of elitism and commercial interest. The wierdest thing is, I don't think that it's even intentional, it seems to be just a side effect of a poor understanding of the way thay modern web communities work.

Maybe soon I'll just stop winging about the fact that I don't like what other people are doing and just do something better myself. I see that someone owns www.patternlanguage.org. Do you know what's going on there?

thats enough ranting for now.

yours,

Craig Ambrose

Michael Saunby 11:28:40 2/14/2003
Do others here have any thoughts on how housing influences happiness?

A recent UK government report of life-satisfaction http://www.strategy.gov.uk/reports/reports.shtml suggests several influences on our personal happiness, from genetics to wealth, education and relationships. There are some interest figures in the report relating to the significance people give to housing for their satisfaction in everyday life. It seems when asked people place it below leisure activities, but when measured (I'm not sure quite how) it comes out much higher - only family life, marriage and financial situation are more important. Which when you look at page 46 and see that time spent on care of our houses comes second only to child care and exceeds the time spent on feeding ourselves it's no wonder.

It would seem there is great potential to improve quality of life through better housing and that this is already being undertaken by ordinary people in their own time. The document suggests that in the UK the value of this unpaid work is something like £175 billion! Globally this must make looking after our homes one of the most important industries in the world.

The question though is - "Do we get £175 billion in satisfaction for our efforts?"

Michael Saunby From Okehampton, Devon, England

Tiana Diehl 13:51:22 1/20/2003
We are building a home in Spring of 2004, and I am delighted to have the chance at implementing these patterns, which have been tickling around inside my head for years. In each home I have lived in, I have noted the characteristics that improved our quality of life, and those which made me curiously depressed...

I have wanted to build residences since I was four years old. I have a passion for throwing out the notions of 'less expensive=poor design', 'one basic bungalow/split level will suit any kind of family', and that merely covering the basic space needs of people will result in a livable space.

The home we currently live in has some lovely qualities. We enter from a large entrance deck at the west (back) side of the house. The outside door opens directly into the kitchen, which is quite inconvenient for a family of 6, but we're greeted with sunlight from the kitchen's full-length patio doors as well as from the living rooms tall windows, directly across. My husband said as soon as he saw the quality of light in those two spaces, he knew it would be a good place.

The home we're planning to build is a monolithic dome home. Have any PL people been involved with the construction of a home like this? I am confident in my current design (which is nearing the 'professional stamp of approval' stage), that we will have a light-filled, cozy, varied interior. I am having issues with the exterior, though. The exterior rooms will be lovely - my concern is with the immediate transition between the dome and the outside. There will be no wings extending from the dome; on the basement level (3' below grade), a sunken & grade level patio with terraced beds, but beyond that is a required flood-proofing berm.

I am most certainly not expecting free design advice - I was hoping to make contact with some people here who may have actually worked on a similar project.

I look forward to visiting this site again; the do-it-yourself tools would be very helpful in my design process.

Tiana Diehl From Lorette, Manitoba, Canada

Vasko Drogriski 17:30:33 12/07/2002
Hi Chriss,

The question of time is interesting when it comes to life.

If you are talking about real communities, they take hundreds of years to organically grow.

Designing a house can take an hour or two, in some cases even less. l have found that the most succesful designs, ironically, have been generated in the least time, often, and in the most dificult contexts.

From my experience in Melbourne, and particularly amongst migrant working class clients, there is a suspicion, a healthy one, about good design taking a long time.

Don't forget, everyone can design... as most people are in the first instance more or less in contact and know their own needs.
It's not a huge step to take the clients brief, or their needs, into a pattern language for the project... most of the design information is, including the solutions and the spaces, within the clients own brief, or their expressed needs.

Money?
It probably does not boil down to an idividual viewpoint of money, but it probably is more related to culture and class.
Those who are more willing to spend money on improving their life, generally have more money to spend, not only on design but across many areas of their lives...
Those who are least willing to spend money on design, generally are also those who have least money to spend. l believe that although this is less true in western countries, it is more true on a world scale. It is also true in newly developing western countries.

In the example l gave of my experience with migrant working class clients... it is true that most now could afford to pay for design but they don't for many reasons. Two that stand out for me are:
1. Design does not give them the 'bang for their buck', where mental labour is undervalued in comparison to physical labour. They would pay you to dig a trench but not to design their home.
2. Most migrant working class clients come from peasant villages where they built their own homes... this was so for as far as they could remember... their were no architects.

People who come from post industrial societies, anglo's, as clients fall into two categories, when it comes to paying for design.
1. Those who can't afford design
2. Those who can afford it but can't build. The 'pattern languages' have been lost. They were lost through the industrial process.

Look around in your community and see who pays for design and who doesn't. l think you will find that it is not just individual pespctive, but there will be some common patterns. l would be interested to hear your observations.

Vasko Drogriski From Melbourne, Australia

vasko 22:39:38 9/15/2002
Hi Bella,

In response to some of your questions and comments;

1. l have not seen 'hard' evidence by others of PL reducing costs.
My understanding so far, of PL, is that the method and application of PL improves the meaning and increases the 'density' of built spaces... because of the way the patterns overlap, and because PL are based on needs rather than on current fashion.
From this angle, it can be claimed that PL do save costs.
This claim however is made by every other architectural firm... that architecure and architects, through expertise of design can yield optimum levels of usable space for construction costs. In reality, this is often not the case.

PL do provide a common language... particularyl if the brief for the project can be developed into a pattern language for the project.

The time savings of PL are that time 'wasted' in design is reduced because the PL are allowing 're-use' of experience and ideas, rather than re-inventing the wheel.
As a friend of mine once put it "architects, too much of the time are trying to invent a new roof (as and example), where, every possible roof that can be built, has been built".

From responses by clients, it seems that clients are pleased with the particular approach that PL use, as well as the built product that they end up with.
What is particularly revealing is the amount of clients who come unsatisfied with previous designs of architects and then are really pleased after re-designing their home by using PL.

2. l have some of my own personal ideas about why l think PL are not in more widespread use, but l do not know for a fact... or from any long experience.
Clients are more interested in PL than directors of architectural firms as they have more interest in their homes being designed to suit theur own needs.
Architects and directors are less interested as the profession tries to mystify architecture... a bit like keeping a secret recipe a secret, so that it can be sold.
Because PL are based on simple rules, there is a rejection on the grounds that they do not allow individual creativity. This is a serious misunderstanding about the role of rules in PL.
l have found that the rules infact allow greater creativity, as well as incredible flexibility in design. Clients can also relate to this, as well as the fact that PL rules talk about real problems and propose real solutions.

l think that PL do have a feminine quality. l do not know whether women are more 'predisposed' to PL because of this.
Many of the families l have worked with in design, the women have taken leading responsiblity and involvement in the design process and often the construction stage too.
The emphasis is in the connectivity of the patterns, particularly the connectivity of patterns dealing with external (garden) spaces and internal patterns of the building dealing with circulation.

The question of why patterns are not in more widespread use needs also to be answered together with other related questions about architecture and building.

The example of Henley Homes using bad patterns rather than good patterns is one example of this. It probably is cheaper for Henley to use foil rather than warm by the sun. The context that they do this in is big and deep.
As a starting point, town planning and the layout of streets and subdivisions ignore most things that would make the implementation of good patterns possible.
Town planning is in turn influenced by other things ie: economics and industry, politics and culture. As town planning design is not based on a wholistic approach itself, ultimately the problem becomes one of bad patterns begetting bad patterns.

Can you elaborate on no 3.

For your interest in PL in business setting you might be interested in www.assai.com.au.

David Week is a Sydney based architect who has a very interesting site and company which uses pattern language as their core approach to business strategy.

Talk to you again.

vasko From Melbourne, Australia

Bella Cranmore 19:43:34 9/09/2002
I have been reading the past entries in this discussion with interest.

I am currently exploring the use of patterns and pattern languages within business settings- specifically looking at their potential in capturing and communicating knowledge with regards to ICT (information and communication technology) design and implementation.

Reading up on the work of Alexander and others, a number of interesting questions have arisen that you may be able to offer some further insights into:

1. In your experiences how do patterns really improve architecture? From the discussions that have arisen the main benefits of patterns appear to be their role in providing a lingua franca and facilitating communication with end users and customers. However is there any evidence of patterns providing ‘hard’ benefits i.e. reducing costs, and speeding up the design and implementation process- both in physical architecture and software design? Within the context of software design are the end users of the software really more satisfied, is the software easier and more pleasant to use?

2. Why have patterns and pattern languages been largely rejected in their field of origin, i.e. physical architecture? Vasko do you have any further insights into this area? Why are clients attracted to APL and not the directors of architectural companies? I am interested in your comment hinting that women may be more predisposed to pattern languages! I am also interested in your comments on the prevalence of ‘bad’ patterns due to their published and ‘gospel’ status, and also the bureaucratic trappings of a capitalist society. The example of Henley Homes using ‘bad’ patterns is notable. Would it not be cheaper for them to exploit nature’s central heating by building north facing living areas instead of forking out for additional insulation and aluminium lining? However I presume that there is some sort of economic incentive to ignore this pattern- presumably they can build more houses on any given plot of land and increase the returns on their investment?

3. How can patterns be validated? Many sources refer to observable application in three different contexts. However, how do you define ‘different’ contexts?

I appreciate that I have asked a lot of questions that do not have simple answers, but look forward in engaging in active discussion with those interested J

I would also be interested in collaborating in writing a pattern language with anyone interested as it may strengthen my understanding of what makes a useful pattern and how they can be utilised to positive effect.

Bella Cranmore From WA, Australia

vasko drogriski 02:44:14 6/03/2002
Hi there Pronob,

They all sound interesting. How will the toolkit be used on the forum?

Regarding the future of the forum, or its best use, my own feelings are these:

1) That the forum is primarily best as a linking tool for people, nationally and internationally. So in that sense, it can assist as an organisational tool.

2) That the forum has limitations as a tool for ongoing education, particularly around heavier theoretical material which needs "fleshing" out and a more disciplinary approach to study and discussion.

3) As entries can only be brief, and the particular format of this way of communicating with one another has the tendency to limit the amount of people who can join in, then only brief discussion can be held... which makes it probably more suitable for information sharing and informing one another about particular issues and our interests.

Having said this, l am still interested in your suggestions, particularly being able to collect or build pattern languages... so how is the best way to do this... the forum, a parallel forum, (possibly your own site, almost as an attachment to this one??? l don't know).

My own particular special interest is:

1) An actual pattern group or groups on the ground. The groups can act as support groups for people interested in patterns, to promote and help each other to learn about pattern languages, and to help people like yourself in taking pattern languages to educational institutions. As well, they can be study groups... around any topics, for example we can discuss the Nature of Order once we have all read it.
The groups can also be multi-disciplinary and need not be limited to any particular field.

2) Actual projects on the ground. There is a fair criticism of groups and associations, that they tend to fizz out if they are not part of any real outcomes on the ground.
So examples of patterns projects do themselves create networks, and vice versa. This can be done in our workplaces. For example l could use the pattern languages with my clients as an axample of patterns used for the design of a buildable house.
In this respect, a patterns group could be useful for someone like me for advice on how to implement the projects.

Going back to the PLOPs conference... for me admittedly it was too narrowly focussed and specific to the programming field.

l was interested however in Jim Copliens idea of joining forces, and making groups more multi-disciplinary.
This can also help to bring in people from outside the professions that we are involved in.

Again having said all this, l guess the forum can be used in any way that people want really... it need not be limited to any particular use... it can be different things to different people.

l'll pause for now and wait for any response.

Talk to you again.

vasko drogriski From Melbourne, Australia

Pronob Pal 20:00:42 4/27/2002
Matt,
I like your comment about "incredibly beauty that is to be seen in all living systems is the flowering of the innate creativity" This is interesting in reference to my open question. Say, you show a 2 year old first time in her life something like a nice art work or some thing natural like a big flower and you ask " do you like it" or "do you want it"?. Most of the time the answer will be "yes". Now assuming the child has never seen the art piece or flower before-the fact is the child appreciates something in it innately, without learning from anyone.. I make the point that the child has somehow identified with the object. Our ability to absorb "knowledge" I believe is something innate in us through some sort of identification with the object and process we develop the knowledge about. As we are slowly brought into the social education system this ability to identify with the knowledge is more and more overlooked and ignored. This is very much enhanced by the presnt reductionist approach to analyse everything into its pieces
My approach is if we can some how bring this innate ability in us to play a role in our society -our every day transactions, then it will simply bring in a new dimension in society and bring in new ways for education as well. Fact is although our social and education system might have had ignored this human ability -it is none the less still there with every human being.
I have sometimes tried to find a pattern for this innate human ability to absorb the knowledge. I have found it very closely associated with human creativity . I have found it ingrained in our spiritual rituals. No matter what kind of relegion or spiritual descipline you consider,probably you shall find the following pattern :
Pattern: Spiritual Prayer
Scene1: Stillness and Concentration
Here the person prepares for the spiritual session -the person takes him/herself to a location and environment where he/she will not be interrupted and shall be feeling connected to immediate surrounding.
Scene2: Admission of Helplessness and Humility, Openness.
Here the person opens up and admits he/she has got very limited knowledge in the field he/she currently in and is tied to all sorts of circumstances and implores for help.
Scene 3 : Aspiration
Here the person is aspiring to understand things in his/her field he/she has not understood yet and somehow surrenders to the process with a belief that the prayer will be granted.
Depending on the particular relegion or spiritual system there can be other scenes in the sequence. Also there will be variations [ change of focus] in the scenes depending on the personality and ofcourse what other patterns the person has been influenced by at that moment.
But the point is this is a very human sequence that is been practised for ages in all sorts of culture and I beleive it will have some empirical evidence that it enhances our innate ability to absorb knowledge and act differently in our own circumstances.
So my refernce to knowledge was this kind of knowledge and understanding. The fact that this knowledge can be gained without doing any reductionist experiment while our sense of logic and scientific approach remain intact points to the fact that these knowledge can and do coexist in our society while we carry out all other social ,business and scientific transactions objectively.
Now it is possible this kind of knowledge adds something to the human being in the physical sense. That was my open question: do you believe that such knowledge can have a existence in the space in and/or around the human being?

You might be wondering what all these have to do with how we design our computer systems. In that respect your remark on the adoptive-systems was interesting -which I shall take up shortly in another discussion.
Shall speak to you soon.

Matt Farrelly 02:36:12 4/26/2002
Hi all,
Pronob, I am interested in your question? In fact as I have been thinking about your model of patterns and scenes over the last couple of days, it occurred to me that what you’re describing is like a “complex adaptive system” to use the jargon of complexity theorists. If I understand you correctly (and I’m probably over simplifying you model here), your system processes scenes and compares them to an internal memory of known patterns and then creates or modifies its patterns-scenes memory and uses the stored patterns to act appropriately in the scene. This means that your system learns and the pattern is a representation of that learning.

It is a characteristic of adaptive systems that they learn and modify their behaviour according their environment so in that sense the adaptive system obtains “knowledge” or at least information about its environment. An adaptive doesn’t have to be human. For example an ant colony is an example of a complex adaptive system it learns the location of food and somehow has the knowledge to direct individual ants to collect the food. Having said that I’m a bit uncomfortable with the term knowledge since to be it implies understanding and I’m not sure that an ant colony understands what it is doing the way that humans do.

The reason I think all this is interesting and relevant is that it occurred to me that CA’s 15 properties are for systems that have “life”. While CA does not appear to be primarily concerned specifically with biologically living organisms (I could be wrong here I haven’t yet read his book) his properties could be applied as descriptions for biologically living systems which are also complex adaptive systems. So what he appears to have done is create an abstract description for attributes of complex adaptive systems. This could be very useful to all those people who are trying to create artificial adaptive systems using computers, like you Pronob, in that if those system do not have the 15 properties they will probably never successfully be truly adaptive, and in that sense never truly living, systems.

Now that I re-read your question Pronob I’m not sure all this what you had in mind? I do believe that life in the universe, in all its forms, is characterised by its ability to learn from its environment and that the incredibly beauty that is to be seen in all living systems is the flowering of the innate creativity of the universe itself.

Vasko I agree with you that something is very wrong in our time we do seem to be very disconnected from nature. My personal explanation for this, for what it is worth, is that the incredible explosion of human knowledge over the past 100 years or so has thrown our relationship with the natural systems out of their old equilibrium’s. To make things worse, things are changing so fast that it is hard to see any new equilibrium forming for some time to come so it will be hard to find new patterns that last. However I believe that CA’s work may be a big help.

Anyway that’s all from me for now.

Have a good weekend everyone.
